	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Germanic peoples</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the <a href="/wiki/Ethnolinguistic_group" title="Ethnolinguistic group">ethnolinguistic group</a>. For the ethnic group of Germany, see <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">A group of northern European tribes in Roman times</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:rgb(220,245,220);padding-left:0.4em;text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-European studies</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<dl><dt><i>Scholars</i></dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Marija_Gimbutas" title="Marija Gimbutas">Marija Gimbutas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/J._P._Mallory" title="J. P. Mallory">J. P. Mallory</a></li></ul>
<dl><dt><i>Institutes</i></dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Studies_in_Indo-European" title="Copenhagen Studies in Indo-European">Copenhagen Studies in Indo-European</a></li></ul>
<dl><dt><i>Publications</i></dt></dl>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Indo-European_Culture" title="Encyclopedia of Indo-European Culture">Encyclopedia of Indo-European Culture</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Horse,_the_Wheel_and_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="The Horse, the Wheel and Language">The Horse, the Wheel and Language</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Indo-European_Studies" title="Journal of Indo-European Studies">Journal of Indo-European Studies</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Indogermanisches_etymologisches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Indogermanisches etymologisches Wörterbuch">Indogermanisches etymologisches Wörterbuch</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_Etymological_Dictionary" title="Indo-European Etymological Dictionary">Indo-European Etymological Dictionary</a></i></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Indo-European_topics" title="Template:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indo-European_topics" title="Template talk:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indo-European_topics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Germanic peoples</b> (also called <a href="/wiki/Teutons" title="Teutons">Teutons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebian</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gaut" title="Gaut">Gothic</a> in older literature) were a collection of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a> of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern European</a> origin identified by Roman-era authors as distinct from neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Celtic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic peoples">Celtic peoples</a>, and identified in modern scholarship as speakers, at least for the most part, of early <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram199712_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram199712-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> population is believed to have emerged during the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a>, which developed out of the <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_Culture#Swedish-Norwegian_Battle_Axe_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Corded Ware Culture">Battle Axe culture</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Europe:_The_Germans_and_Huns,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Europe:_The_Germans_and_Huns,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> various Germanic tribes began a southward expansion at the expense of <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic peoples</a>, which led to <a href="/wiki/Germanic_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Wars">centuries of sporadic violent conflict</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>. It is from Roman authors that the term "Germanic" originated. The decisive victory of <a href="/wiki/Arminius" title="Arminius">Arminius</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Teutoburg_Forest" title="Battle of the Teutoburg Forest">Battle of the Teutoburg Forest</a> in 9 CE is believed to have prevented the eventual <a href="/wiki/Romanization_(cultural)" title="Romanization (cultural)">Romanization</a> of the Germanic peoples, and has therefore been considered a turning point in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">world history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> Germanic tribes settled the entire Roman frontier along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, and some established close relations with the Romans, often serving as <a href="/wiki/Mardonius_(philosopher)" title="Mardonius (philosopher)">royal tutors</a> and mercenaries, sometimes even rising to the highest offices in the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military of ancient Rome">Roman military</a>. Meanwhile, Germanic tribes expanded into <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> subdued the local <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">nomads</a> and came to dominate the <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Steppe" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontic Steppe">Pontic Steppe</a>, simultaneously launching sea expeditions into the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200982–83_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200982–83-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram198886–89_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram198886–89-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The westward expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> into Europe in the late 4th century CE <a href="/wiki/Migration_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Migration period">pushed</a> many Germanic tribes into the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. Their vacated lands were filled by <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>. Many of these territories were <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">reclaimed</a> in following centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Other tribes <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">settled</a> <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and became known as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>. With the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Collapse of the Western Roman Empire">collapse of the Western Roman Empire</a>, a series of <a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">Germanic kingdoms</a> emerged, of which <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> gained a dominant position. This kingdom formed the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, who was officially recognized by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> in 800 CE. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">North Germanic</a> seafarers, commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, embarked on a massive expansion which led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">Kievan Rus'</a> and their settlement of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic Ocean</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Vinland" title="Vinland">North America</a>. Historical <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>, the indigenous religion of the Germanic peoples, ended with <a href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_the_Germanic_peoples" title="Christianisation of the Germanic peoples">Christianization</a> in the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_religion_and_mythology,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_religion_and_mythology,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Elements of Germanic paganism continued into post-Christianization <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, and today Germanic paganism sees a modern revival in <a href="/wiki/Heathenry_(new_religious_movement)" title="Heathenry (new religious movement)">Heathenry</a>, a <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movement</a>. The descendants of the original Germanic peoples are still known as the Germanic peoples.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnonym">Ethnonym</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germanic">Germanic</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a></div>
<p>In about 222 BCE, the first use of the Latin term "<i>Germani</i>" appears in the <i><a href="/wiki/Fasti_Capitolini" title="Fasti Capitolini">Fasti Capitolini</a></i> inscription <i>de Galleis Insvbribvs et Germ(aneis)</i>. This may simply be referring to <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> or related people; but this may be an inaccurate date since the inscription was erected in about 18 BCE despite referencing an earlier date. The term <i>Germani</i> shows up again, allegedly written by <a href="/wiki/Poseidonios" class="mw-redirect" title="Poseidonios">Poseidonios</a> (from 80 BCE), but is merely a quotation inserted by the author <a href="/wiki/Athenaios" class="mw-redirect" title="Athenaios">Athenaios</a> who wrote much later (around 190 CE).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStümpel193260_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStümpel193260-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Somewhat later, the first surviving detailed discussions of Germani and <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> are those of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, whose memoirs are based on first-hand experience.
</p><p>From Caesar's perspective, <i>Germania</i> was a geographical area of land on the east bank of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> opposite Gaul, which Caesar left outside direct <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> control. This word provides the etymological origin of the modern concept of "<a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>" languages and Germany as a geographical abstraction. For some classical authors Germania also included regions of <a href="/wiki/Sarmatia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarmatia">Sarmatia</a>, as well as an area under Roman control on the west bank of the Rhine. Additionally, in the south there were <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> peoples still living east of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and north of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>. Caesar, <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> and others noted differences of culture which could be found on the east of the Rhine. But the theme of all these cultural references was that this was a wild and dangerous region, less civilized than Gaul, a place that required additional military vigilance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather20125–8_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather20125–8-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar also used the term <i>Germani</i> for a very specific tribal grouping in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Belgic" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgic">Belgic</a> Gaul, west of the Rhine, the largest part of whom were the <a href="/wiki/Eburones" title="Eburones">Eburones</a>. He made clear that he was using the name in the local sense. These are the so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Germani_Cisrhenani" class="mw-redirect" title="Germani Cisrhenani">Germani Cisrhenani</a></i>, whom Caesar believed to be closely related to the peoples east of the Rhine, and descended from immigrants into Gaul.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar201945,_2.4_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar201945,_2.4-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> suggests that this was the original meaning of the word "<i>Germani</i>" – as the name of a single tribal nation west of the Rhine, ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Tungri" title="Tungri">Tungri</a> (who lived in the same area as the earlier <i>Germani</i> reported by Caesar), and not the name of a whole race (<i>gens</i>) as it came to mean. However, Tacitus adds that in due time, the name Germani "gradually acquired a wider usage" and that "once they had got to know the name, they all called themselves Germani."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200938_&#91;Ch._2&#93;_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200938_[Ch._2]-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  Caesar described this group of tribes both as Belgic <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a> and as <i>Germani</i>. Gauls are associated with <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>, and the term <i>Germani</i> is associated with Germanic languages, but Caesar did not discuss languages in detail (though he did say that Belgic Gaul was different from Celtic Gaul in language). The geographer <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> described the place where these people lived as <i>Germania</i>, which according to his accounts was bordered by the Rhine, <a href="/wiki/Vistula" title="Vistula">Vistula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danube_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Danube River">Danube Rivers</a>, but he also circumscribed into Greater <i>Germania</i> an area which included <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">Jutland</a> (Cimbrian peninsula) and an enormous island known as <i><a href="/wiki/Scandia" class="mw-redirect" title="Scandia">Scandia</a></i> (the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Scandinavian peninsula">Scandinavian peninsula</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManco2013207_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManco2013207-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While saying that the Germani had ancestry across the Rhine, Caesar did not describe these tribes as recent immigrants, saying that they had defended themselves some generations earlier from the invading <a href="/wiki/Cimbri" title="Cimbri">Cimbri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teutones" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutones">Teutones</a>. (He thereby distinguished them from the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Aduatuci" class="mw-redirect" title="Aduatuci">Aduatuci</a>, whom he did not call <i>Germani</i>, but who were descended from those Cimbri and Teutones.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar201945–46,_2.4_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar201945–46,_2.4-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It has been claimed, for example by <a href="/wiki/Maurits_Gysseling" title="Maurits Gysseling">Maurits Gysseling</a>, that the place names of this region show evidence of an early presence of Germanic languages, as early as the 2nd century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamarcqRogge199644_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamarcqRogge199644-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Celtic culture and language were however clearly influential also, as can be seen in the tribal name of the Eburones, their kings' names, <a href="/wiki/Ambiorix" title="Ambiorix">Ambiorix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cativolcus" title="Cativolcus">Cativolcus</a>, and also the <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamarcqRogge199647_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamarcqRogge199647-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The etymology of the word <i>Germani</i> is uncertain. The likeliest theory so far proposed is that it comes from a <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> compound of *<i>ger</i> "near" + *<i>mani</i> "men", comparable to <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>ger</i> "near" (prep.), <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> <i>gair</i> "neighbor", <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> <i>gar-</i> (prefix) "near", <i>garach</i> "neighborly".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchulze20014_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchulze20014-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Another Celtic possibility is that the name meant "noisy"; cf. <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>/<a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> <i>garm</i> "shout", Irish <i>gairm</i> "call".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPartridge19661265_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPartridge19661265-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> However, here the vowel does not match, nor does the vowel length (contrast with inscriptional <i>Garmangabi</i> (UK) and <i>Garma</i> Alise, G-257)). Others have proposed a Germanic etymology *<i>gēr</i>-<i>manni</i>, "spear men", cf. <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>ghere</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>Ger</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>geirr</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997245_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997245-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> However, the form <i>gēr</i> (from PGmc *<i>gaizaz</i>) seems far too advanced phonetically for the 1st century, has a long vowel where a short one is expected, and the Latin form has a simplex -<i>n</i>-, not a <a href="/wiki/Geminate" class="mw-redirect" title="Geminate">geminate</a>.
</p><p>The term <i>Germani</i>, therefore, probably applied to a small group of tribes in northeastern Gaul who may or may not have spoken a Germanic language, and whose links to <i>Germania</i> are unclear. It appears that the Germanic tribes did not have a word to describe themselves, although the word <i><a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a></i>, used by Caesar to broadly classify Germanic speakers, was likely Germanic in origin.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> They did however use the term <i><a href="/wiki/Walhaz" title="Walhaz">walhaz</a></i> to describe outsiders (mainly Celts, Romans and <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Roman authors frequently employed the term "<a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a>" from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> derivative <i>barbarus</i> (inherited from the Greek <i>barbaros</i> which means "foreign") when describing Germanic peoples. Such a term presupposed a distinctive Roman intellectual and cultural superiority and their ethnographic treatises on the various barbarian tribes ascribed specific attributes of barbarism to each one so as to delineate the dichotomy between barbarism and civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns200315–16_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns200315–16-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The more the Romans increased their presence along the periphery of their Empire, the more trade and employment for the barbarians became available, resulting in an economic boom along the corridors of the Danube River, which subsequently increased the Roman focus upon the Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2003232–233_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2003232–233-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Use of the modern term German or Germanic is the result of 18th and 19th century classical philology which "envisioned the Germanic language group as occupying a central branch of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language</a> tree."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns200319_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns200319-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teutonic">Teutonic</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Teutons" title="Teutons">Teutons</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodiscus" title="Theodiscus">Theodiscus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Teutonic (disambiguation)">Teutonic (disambiguation)</a></div>
<p>Latin scholars of the 10th century used the adjective <i>teutonicus</i> (a derivative of <a href="/wiki/Teutones" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutones">Teutones</a>) when referencing <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>, which in their vernacular was connoted <i>"Regnum Teutonicum"</i>, for that area and all of its subsequent inhabitants. Modern speakers of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> still use the word "Teutons" to describe Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Historically, the Teutones were only one specific tribe, and may not even have spoken a Germanic language. For example, some scholars postulate that the original Teutonic language may have been a form of Celtic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The source of this confusion, whereby Teutons are lumped into the same category as German-speaking tribes, comes from their contact with the Romans in the 2nd century BCE, when they, along with the Cimbri and the <a href="/wiki/Ambrones" title="Ambrones">Ambrones</a>, led a frightening attack against the Romans. Teuton was the byword the Romans applied to the barbarians from the north and which they used to describe subsequent Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDetwiler19993_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDetwiler19993-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>, who built his career on barbarian antagonists (like many who followed), the Teutones became one of the archetypal enemies of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns200366–67_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns200366–67-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png/220px-Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png/330px-Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png/440px-Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png 2x" data-file-width="2067" data-file-height="2312" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanic_dialects_ca._AD_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One proposed theory for <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> dialect groups and their approximate distribution in northern Europe around <span class="nowrap">1 CE:</span> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Blue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Germanic" title="North Sea Germanic">North Sea Germanic</a> (Ingvaeonic)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Orange; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Weser-Rhine_Germanic" title="Weser-Rhine Germanic">Weser-Rhine Germanic</a>, (Istvaeonic)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Elbe_Germanic" title="Elbe Germanic">Elbe Germanic</a> (Irminonic)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Green; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">East Germanic</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>By the 1st century CE, the writings of <a href="/wiki/Pomponius_Mela" title="Pomponius Mela">Pomponius Mela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cornelius_Tacitus" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaius Cornelius Tacitus">Tacitus</a> indicate a division of Germanic peoples into large groupings who shared ancestry and culture. This division has been appropriated in modern terminology describing the divisions of Germanic languages.
</p><p>Tacitus, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i>, wrote that:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the ancient songs...they celebrate <a href="/wiki/Tuisco" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuisco">Tuisto</a>, an earth-born god. To him they attribute a son, <a href="/wiki/Mannus" title="Mannus">Mannus</a>, the forefather and founder of their people, and to Mannus three sons, after whom were named the <a href="/wiki/Ing%C3%A6vones" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingævones">Ingvaeones</a>, nearest to the Ocean, the <a href="/wiki/Herminones" class="mw-redirect" title="Herminones">Herminones</a> in the interior, and the remainder <a href="/wiki/Ist%C3%A6vones" class="mw-redirect" title="Istævones">Istvaeones</a>.
</p></blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200938_&#91;Ch._2&#93;_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200938_[Ch._2]-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tacitus also specifies that the Suebi are a very large grouping, with many tribes within it, with their own names. The largest, he says, is the <a href="/wiki/Semnones" title="Semnones">Semnones</a>, who he says, "claim that they are the oldest and the noblest of the Suebi."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200957_&#91;Ch._39&#93;_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200957_[Ch._39]-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> He goes on to remark that the <a href="/wiki/Langobardi" class="mw-redirect" title="Langobardi">Langobardi</a> are fewer, but despite being "surrounded by many mighty peoples" they managed to defend themselves "not by submissiveness but by battle and boldness; and in remoter and better defended areas live the <a href="/wiki/Reudigni" title="Reudigni">Reudigni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aviones" class="mw-redirect" title="Aviones">Aviones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglii" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglii">Anglii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varini" class="mw-redirect" title="Varini">Varini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eudoses" class="mw-redirect" title="Eudoses">Eudoses</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Suardones" class="mw-redirect" title="Suardones">Suardones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuithones" title="Nuithones">Nuithones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200958_&#91;Ch._40&#93;_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200958_[Ch._40]-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, on the other hand, names five races of Germans in his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Historia Naturalis</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> not three, by distinguishing the two more easterly blocks of Germans, the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a> and further east the <a href="/wiki/Bastarnae" title="Bastarnae">Bastarnae</a>, who were the first to reach the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and come into contact with Greek civilization. He is also slightly more specific about the position of the Istvaeones, though he also does not name any examples of them:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There are five German races; the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandili</a>, parts of whom are the <a href="/wiki/Burgundiones" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundiones">Burgundiones</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Varini" class="mw-redirect" title="Varini">Varini</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Carini_(Germanic_tribe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Carini (Germanic tribe)">Carini</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gutones" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutones">Gutones</a>: the Ingævones, forming a second race, a portion of whom are the Cimbri, the Teutoni, and the tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Chauci" title="Chauci">Chauci</a>. The Istævones, who join up to the Rhine, and to whom the Cimbri [sic, repeated] belong, are the third race; while the Hermiones, forming a fourth, dwell in the interior, and include the Suevi, the <a href="/wiki/Hermunduri" title="Hermunduri">Hermunduri</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chatti" title="Chatti">Chatti</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cherusci" title="Cherusci">Cherusci</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> and the Peucini, who are also the <a href="/wiki/Bastarnae" title="Bastarnae">Basternæ</a>, adjoining the <a href="/wiki/Dacians" title="Dacians">Daci</a>. The remote Varini are listed by Tacitus as being in the Suebic or Hermionic group by Tacitus, above, but by Pliny in the eastern Vandalic or <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Gothic</a> group, so the two accounts do not match perfectly.
</p></blockquote>
<p>These accounts and others from the period often emphasise that the Suebi and their Hermione kin formed an especially large and mobile nation, which at the time were living mainly near the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a>, both east and west of it, but they were also moving westwards into the lands near the Roman frontier. <a href="/wiki/Pomponius_Mela" title="Pomponius Mela">Pomponius Mela</a> in his slightly earlier <i>Description of the World,</i> places "the farthest people of <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a>, the Hermiones" somewhere to the east of the Cimbri and the Teutones, and further from Rome, apparently on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic sea">Baltic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> however describes the Suebi as going through a period where they were pushed back east by the Romans, in the direction from which they had come:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the nation of the Suevi is the most considerable, as it extends from the Rhine as far as the Elbe, and even a part of them, as the Hermonduri and the Langobardi, inhabit the country beyond the Elbe; but at the present time these tribes, having been defeated, have retired entirely beyond the Elbe.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>By the end of the 5th century the term "Gothic" was used more generally in the historical sources for Pliny's "Vandals" to the east of the Elbe, including not only the Goths and Vandals, but also "the <a href="/wiki/Gepids" title="Gepids">Gepids</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Tisza" title="Tisza">Tisza</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rugians" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugians">Rugians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scirii" title="Scirii">Sciri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burgundians" title="Burgundians">Burgundians</a>, even the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistics">Linguistics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_substrate_hypothesis" title="Germanic substrate hypothesis">Germanic substrate hypothesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Indo-European_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Spread of Indo-European languages">Spread of Indo-European languages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/220px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/330px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG/440px-Old_norse%2C_ca_900.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="909" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_norse,_ca_900.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The approximate extent of Germanic languages in northern Europe in the early 10th century: <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Old_West_Norse" class="mw-redirect" title="Old West Norse">Old West Norse</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9933; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Old_East_Norse" class="mw-redirect" title="Old East Norse">Old East Norse</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff00ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Old_Gutnish" title="Old Gutnish">Old Gutnish</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Continental <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">Old Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a>).</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">Crimean Gothic</a> (<a href="/wiki/East_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="East Germanic">East Germanic</a>)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Linguists postulate that an early proto-Germanic language existed and was distinguishable from the other Indo-European languages as far back as 500 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The earliest known Germanic inscription was found at <a href="/wiki/Negova" title="Negova">Negau</a> (in what is now southern <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>) on <a href="/wiki/Negau_helmet" title="Negau helmet">a bronze helmet</a> dating back to the first century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199912–13_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199912–13-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Some of the other earliest known physical records of the Germanic language appear on stone and wood carvings in <a href="/wiki/Runic" class="mw-redirect" title="Runic">Runic</a> script from around 200 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Runic writing likely disappeared due to the concerted opposition of the Christian Church, which regarded runic text as heathen symbols which supposedly contained inherent magical properties that they associated with the Germanic peoples' pagan past.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalsall198115_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalsall198115-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Unfortunately, this primitive view ignores the abundance of "pious runic writing found on church-related objects" (ranging from inscriptions in the doorways of churches, on church bells and even those found on baptismal fonts) when Christianity was introduced into the Germanic North.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonsen200237_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonsen200237-40">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> An important linguistic step was made by the Christian convert <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">Ulfilas</a>, who became a bishop to the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a> in CE 341; he subsequently invented an alphabet and translated the scriptures from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> into <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, creating the earliest known translation of the Bible into a Germanic language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer201044_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer201044-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From what is known, the early Germanic tribes may have spoken "<a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a> dialects" derived from a common parent language but there are no written records to verify this fact. Despite their common linguistic framework, by the 5th century CE, the Germanic people were linguistically differentiated and could no longer easily comprehend one another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMusset199312–13_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMusset199312–13-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, the line between Germanic languages and <a href="/wiki/Romance_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance language">Romance</a> speakers in central Europe remained at the western mouth of the Rhine river and while Gaul fell under German domination and was firmly settled by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, the linguistic patterns did not move much. Further west and south in Europe-proper, the linguistic presence of the Germanic languages is almost negligible. Despite the fact that the Visigoths <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Kingdom" title="Visigothic Kingdom">ruled</a> what is now <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> for upwards of 250 years, there are almost no recognizable Gothic words borrowed into <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOstler2006307_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOstler2006307-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Germanic tribes moved and interacted over the next centuries, and separate dialects among Germanic languages developed down to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224–225_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalby1999224–225-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Some groups, such as the Suebi, have a continuous recorded existence, and so there is a reasonable confidence that their modern dialects can be traced back to those in classical times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson1992194–195_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson1992194–195-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By extension, but sometimes controversially, the names of the sons of Mannus, Istvaeones, Irminones, and Ingvaeones, are also sometimes used to divide up the medieval and modern <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The more easterly groups such as the Vandals are thought to have been united in the use of <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">East Germanic languages</a>, the most famous of which is Gothic. The dialect of the Germanic people who remained in Scandinavia is not generally called Ingvaeonic, but is classified as <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic</a>, which developed into <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>. Within the West Germanic group, linguists associate the Suebian or Hermionic group with an "<a href="/wiki/Elbe_Germanic" title="Elbe Germanic">Elbe Germanic</a>" which developed into <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a>, including modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOstler2006304–314_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOstler2006304–314-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG/220px-R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG/330px-R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG/440px-R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6kstenen_1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An inscription using <a href="/wiki/Cipher_runes" title="Cipher runes">cipher runes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Elder_Futhark" title="Elder Futhark">Elder Futhark</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Futhark" title="Younger Futhark">Younger Futhark</a>, on the 9th-century <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B6k_Runestone" class="mw-redirect" title="Rök Runestone">Rök Runestone</a> in Sweden</div></div></div>
<p>More speculatively, given the lack of any such clear explanation in any classical source, modern linguists sometimes designate the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish language</a> (and its descendant <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>) as <a href="/wiki/Istvaeonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Istvaeonic">Istvaeonic</a>, although the geographical term "<a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a>-<a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> Germanic" is often preferred. However, the classical "<i>Germani</i>" near the Rhine, to whom the term was originally applied by Caesar, may not have even spoken Germanic languages, let alone a language recognizably ancestral to modern Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWightman198512–14_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWightman198512–14-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The close relatives of Dutch, <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a>, English and <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian</a>, are in fact sometimes designated as <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingvaeonic">Ingvaeonic</a>, or alternatively, "North-Sea Germanic". Frankish, (and later Dutch, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourgish" title="Luxembourgish">Luxembourgish</a> and the Frankish dialects of German in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>) has continuously been intelligible to some extent with both "Ingvaeonic" Low German, and some "Suebian" <a href="/wiki/High_German" class="mw-redirect" title="High German">High German</a> dialects, with which they form a spectrum of continental dialects. All these dialects or languages appear to have formed by the mixing of migrating peoples after the time of Caesar. So it is not clear if these medieval dialect divisions correspond to any mentioned by Tacitus and Pliny. Indeed, in Tacitus (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus:text:1999.02.0083:chapter=40&amp;highlight=anglii">Tac. Ger. 40</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200958_&#91;Ch._40&#93;_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200958_[Ch._40]-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" class="mw-redirect" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Claudius Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglii" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglii">Anglii</a>, ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, are designated as being a Suebic tribe.
</p><p>By 500 CE, the West Germanic speakers had apparently developed a distinct language continuum with extensive loaning from Latin (due to their ongoing contact with the Romans), whereas the <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">East Germanic languages</a> were dying out.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> West Germanic languages include German, <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>, Dutch, <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, Luxemburgish, Frisian, and English. Combined, these languages are today spoken as a native tongue by more than 450 million people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> North Germanic languages are <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalby1999225_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalby1999225-50">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Roughly 20 million people currently speak the North Germanic languages as their native tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006300-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Later manifestations of the West Germanic languages and their pursuant typological characteristics are due in part to the activities of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> where trade necessitated a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> from the mainland of Scandinavia all along the navigable shores of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and within the Baltic Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordic_Bronze_Age.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/220px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/330px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/440px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png 2x" data-file-width="657" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nordic_Bronze_Age.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a> culture, around 1200 BCE</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">Corded Ware culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a></div>
<p>Archaeological and linguistic evidence from a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a> indicates that a common material culture existed between the Germanic tribes that inherited the southern regions of Scandinavia, along with the <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a> area and the area of what is now <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann2004109_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinderHilgemann2004109-52">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Additional archaeological remnants from the Iron Age society that once existed in nearby <a href="/wiki/Wessenstedt" title="Wessenstedt">Wessenstedt</a> also show traces of this culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Exactly how these cultures interacted remains a mystery but the migrations of early proto-Germanic peoples are discernible from the remaining evidence of prehistoric cultures in Hügelgräber, <a href="/wiki/Urnfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Urnfield">Urnfield</a>, and <a href="/wiki/La_Tene" class="mw-redirect" title="La Tene">La Tene</a>. Climatic change between 850 BCE to 760 BCE in Scandinavia and "a later and more rapid one around 650 BCE might have triggered migrations to the coast of eastern Germany and further toward the Vistula.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg/220px-Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg/330px-Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg/440px-Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2080" data-file-height="1620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Solvognen_-_Do_2010_1276.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The gilded side of the <a href="/wiki/Trundholm_sun_chariot" title="Trundholm sun chariot">Trundholm sun chariot</a></div></div></div>
<p>The cultural phase of the late Bronze Age and early Iron Age in Europe (c. 1200–600 BCE in temperate continental areas), known in contemporary terms as the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> expanded from the south into this area and brought the early Germanic peoples under the influence of early Celtic (or pre Celtic) culture between 1200 BCE to 600 BCE, whereupon they began extracting <a href="/wiki/Bog_iron" title="Bog iron">bog iron</a> from the available ore in <a href="/wiki/Peat_bogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bogs">peat bogs</a>. This ushered in the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age">Pre-Roman Iron Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Stretching from central <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> all the way to western <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> and then from the Alps to central <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> also constructed sophisticated structures and the archaeological remains across parts of France, Germany and Hungary suggest their trade networks along the North Atlantic, Baltic Sea and up and down central Europe's river valleys were fairly elaborate as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011309–316_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011309–316-53">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest sites at which Germanic peoples <i>per se</i> have been documented are in Northern Europe, in what now constitutes the plains of Denmark and southern Sweden. However, in even this region, the population had been, according to Waldman &amp; Mason, "remarkably stable" – as far back as <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> times, when humans first began controlling their environment through the use of agriculture and the domestication of animals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006296–297_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006296–297-54">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Given this stability, the population of the region necessarily preceded the arrival in Europe of the precursors of the Germanic languages – which most likely began with the <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">Corded Ware culture</a>.
</p><p>During the 2nd millennium BCE, the so-called Nordic Bronze Age expanded eastward into the adjacent regions between the estuaries of the Elbe and <a href="/wiki/Oder" title="Oder">Oder</a> rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury20005_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury20005-55">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As early as 750 BCE, archeological evidence gives the impression that the proto-Germanic population was becoming more uniform in its culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Germanic peoples at the time inhabited southern Scandinavia and the Northern Sea and Baltic coasts from modern-day <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> to the Vistula.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_languages:_The_Emergence_of_Germanic_Languages,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_languages:_The_Emergence_of_Germanic_Languages,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-56">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> As this population grew, it migrated south-west, into coastal floodplains due to the exhaustion of the soil in its original settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerhart200681–82_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerhart200681–82-57">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Iron_Age">Early Iron Age</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age">Pre-Roman Iron Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dejbjerg_wagon,_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg/220px-Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg/330px-Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg/440px-Dejbjerg_wagon%2C_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dejbjerg_wagon,_Nationalmuseet_Copenhagen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Dejbjerg wagon, National Museum of Denmark</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png/200px-Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png/300px-Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png/400px-Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png 2x" data-file-width="545" data-file-height="515" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Archeological_cultures_in_Northern_and_Central_Europe_at_the_late_pre-Roman_Iron_Age.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Archeological_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Archeological culture">Archeological cultures</a> of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> in the late <a href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age">Pre-Roman Iron Age</a>:<br /> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B31010; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jastorf_culture" title="Jastorf culture">Jastorf culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #18AA00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Nordic culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #E5CD4E; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Harpstedt-Nienburger group</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFA401; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #BBDD00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Przeworsk_culture" title="Przeworsk culture">Przeworsk culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #20FF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/House_Urns_culture" title="House Urns culture">House Urns culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F36B78; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Balt" class="mw-redirect" title="Balt">Eastern Balt (Forest Zone) culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #CA34EE; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Balt" class="mw-redirect" title="Balt">Western Balt culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #40D0C8; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Zarubintsy_culture" title="Zarubintsy culture">Zarubintsy culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #48435A; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Estonian group</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #EE0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Gubin culture</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B18300; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Oksywie_culture" title="Oksywie culture">Oksywie culture</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #CAAB92; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Thracian_language" title="Thracian language">Thracian group</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F8FB26; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Poienesti-Lukasevka culture</div></div></div></div>
<p>By approximately 250 BCE, additional expansion further southwards into central Europe had begun to take place and five general groups of Germanic people emerged, each employing distinct linguistic dialects but sharing similar language innovations — they are distinguished from one another as: <i>North Germanic</i> in southern Scandinavia; <i>North Sea Germanic</i> in the regions along the North Sea and in the Jutland peninsula NW Europe, which forms the mainland of Denmark together with the north German state of Schleswig-Holstein; <i><a href="/wiki/Weser-Rhine_Germanic" title="Weser-Rhine Germanic">Weser-Rhine Germanic</a></i> along the middle Rhine and Weser river (which empties into the North Sea near Bremerhaven); <i>Elbe Germanic</i> spoken by the people living directly along the middle Elbe river; and <i>East Germanic</i> between the middle of the Oder and the Vistula rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concomitantly, during the 2nd century BCE the advent of the Celts of Halstatt and <a href="/wiki/La_Tene" class="mw-redirect" title="La Tene">La Tene</a> arose in nearby territories further west but the interactions between the early Germanic people and the Celts is thought to have been minimal based on the linguistic evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerhart200667_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerhart200667-59">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Despite the absence of the Celtic influence further eastwards, there are a number of Celtic loanwords in Proto-Germanic, which at the very least indicates contact between the people of Gaul and the early Germanic cultures that resided along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> river.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWell1996603–611_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWell1996603–611-60">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, material objects such as metal ornaments and pottery found near the areas east of the lower Rhine are connoted as <a href="/wiki/Jastorf_culture" title="Jastorf culture">Jastorf</a> in nomenclature and are characteristically distinguishable from the Celtic objects found further west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoguckiCrabtree2003152_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoguckiCrabtree2003152-61">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is not clear if the first occurrence of the term <i>Germani</i> in Roman ethnography is either a reference to Germanic or Celtic according to modern linguists, but it is probable that the clear geographic demarcation appearing between the two peoples may have been made for the sake of political convenience by Caesar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997222_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalloryAdams1997222-62">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Caesar described some tribes more distinctly than others but generally considered most of them as being from Germanic stock. However, the archaeological evidence in some of the regions creates an ethnographic problem in clearly delineating the indigenous people based strictly on Roman classification. Nonetheless, there are scholars who assert that there was an eventual linguistic "<a href="/wiki/Germanization" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanization">Germanization</a>" that occurred during the 1st century BCE through something they call the "elite-dominance" model.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHachmannKossackKuhn1962183–212_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHachmannKossackKuhn1962183–212-63">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Archaeologists are unable to make definitive judgments which accord the observations of the Roman writer Tacitus. Enough cultural absorption between the various Germanic people occurred that geographically defining the extent of pre-Roman Germanic territory is nearly impossible from a classification standpoint.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerhart2006175–176_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerhart2006175–176-64">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some recognizable trends in the archaeological records exist, as it is known that, generally, West Germanic people while still migratory, were more geographically settled, whereas the East Germanic peoples remained transitory for a longer period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury20006_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury20006-65">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Three settlement patterns and solutions come to the fore, the first of which is the establishment of an agricultural base in a region which allowed them to support larger populations; second, the Germanic peoples periodically cleared forests to extend the range of their pasturage; thirdly (and the most frequent occurrence), they often emigrated to other areas as they exhausted the immediately available resources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury20006–7_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury20006–7-66">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> War and conquest followed as the Germanic people migrated bringing them into direct conflict with the Celts who were forced to either Germanize or migrate elsewhere as a result. Evidence suggest that these were Germanized rather than displaced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermany:_Ancient_History,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermany:_Ancient_History,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-67">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> West Germanic peoples eventually settled in central Europe and became more accustomed to agriculture and it is those people that are described by Caesar and Tacitus. Meanwhile, the East Germanic people continued their migratory habits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury20007–9_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury20007–9-68">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Roman writers characteristically organized and classified people and it may very well have been deliberate on their part to recognize the tribal distinctions of the various Germanic people so as to pick out known leaders and exploit these differences for their benefit. For the most part however, these early Germanic people shared a basic culture, operated similarly from an economic perspective, and were not nearly as differentiated as the Romans implied. In fact, the Germanic tribes are hard to distinguish from the Celts on many accounts simply based on archaeological records.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006301_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006301-69">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_contacts">Earliest contacts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pytheas_itineraire.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pytheas_itineraire.png/240px-Pytheas_itineraire.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pytheas_itineraire.png/360px-Pytheas_itineraire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pytheas_itineraire.png/480px-Pytheas_itineraire.png 2x" data-file-width="547" data-file-height="657" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pytheas_itineraire.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The travels of <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bastarnae" title="Bastarnae">Bastarnae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scirii" title="Scirii">Scirii</a></div>
<p>One of the earliest known written records of the Germanic world in classical times was in the lost work of <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a>. Pytheas traveled to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, some time in the late 4th century BCE, and his observations about the geographical environment, traditions and culture of the northern European populations became a central source of information for later historians – often the only source.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup> Authors such as Strabo, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus" class="mw-redirect" title="Diodorus">Diodorus</a> cite Pytheas in disbelief, although Pytheas' observations appear to have been accurate. Though Pytheas was not the first Mediterranean to explore those lands (note for example <a href="/wiki/Himilco" title="Himilco">Himilco</a> (5th century BCE), and possibly <a href="/wiki/Massaliote_Periplus" title="Massaliote Periplus">Phoenicians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Massaliote_Periplus" title="Massaliote Periplus">Tartessians</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201; 6th century BCE</span>), his became the first substantial surviving description of these populations.
