Much of the Germanic peoples' early history enters into view through Pytheas, particularly since he was also possibly the first to distinguish the <i>Germanoi</i> people of northern and central Europe as distinct from the <i>Keltoi</i> people further west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsborne200838_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsborne200838-71">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe20116–8_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe20116–8-72">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Along with the records of a couple of other classical writers (namely <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> (2nd century BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a> (c. 135 BCE – c. 51 BCE), the work of Pytheas on the Celts and early Germans influenced scores of future geographers, historians and ethnographers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns200351–52_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns200351–52-73">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%;&#32;;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Bastarnae" title="Bastarnae">Bastarnae</a>, the bravest nation of all<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDenniston1962369_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDenniston1962369-74">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>An early Germanic people known as the <a href="/wiki/Bastarnae" title="Bastarnae">Bastarnae</a> were identified by Roman authors and were allegedly the first to reach the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a>, living in the area north of the Danube's mouth in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. They resided primarily in the territory east of the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Dniester_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Dniester River">Dniester River</a> valley and the delta of the Danube in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and are considered the easternmost of the Germanic tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661-75">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The Bastarnae are mentioned in historical sources going back as far as the 3rd century BCE all the way through the 4th century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199924_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199924-76">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In 201–202 BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonians</a> under the leadership of King <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V</a>, conscripted the Bastarnae as soldiers to fight against the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Macedonian_War" title="Second Macedonian War">Second Macedonian War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661-75">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> They remained a presence in that area until late in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> while some settled on <a href="/wiki/Peuce_Island" title="Peuce Island">Peuce Island</a> at the mouth of the Danube on the Black Sea which is why the name Peucini is also associated with the Bastarnae.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661-75">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> King <a href="/wiki/Perseus_of_Macedon" title="Perseus of Macedon">Perseus</a> enlisted the service of the Bastarnae in 171–168 BCE to fight the <a href="/wiki/Third_Macedonian_War" title="Third Macedonian War">Third Macedonian War</a>. By 29 BCE, they were subdued by the Romans and those that remained began merging with various tribes of Goths into the second century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661-75">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Historian Thomas Burns references the Bastarnae but only as an aside from the Latin poet <a href="/wiki/Claudian" title="Claudian">Claudian</a>, claiming that they were among "the oldest of the various <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythian</a> people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1994103_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1994103-77">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Burns further elaborates in stating that there are no "specific references" to the Bastarnae and that remarks about them by Claudian and later third century writers "must give us pause" for the mention of such people might merely have been a "convenient poetic device."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns1994103_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns1994103-77">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Heather" title="Peter Heather">Peter Heather</a> disagrees with this position and identifies the Bastarnae as one of the Germanic tribes and asserts that they once "dominated substantial tracts of territory at the mouth of the Danube."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather200549_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather200549-78">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Along similar lines, the late classical scholar, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Mommsen" title="Theodor Mommsen">Theodor Mommsen</a>, recognized the Bastarnae and placed them in the geographic regions of Moldavia and <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Bessarabia</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMommsen1968229_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMommsen1968229-79">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup> This is the same region where Tacitus placed them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1998184_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1998184-81">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Another historian of antiquity, <a href="/wiki/J._B._Bury" title="J. B. Bury">J. B. Bury</a>, counted the Bastarnae along with the Goths, Vandals, Gepids, Burgundians, <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rugians" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugians">Rugians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heruls" class="mw-redirect" title="Heruls">Heruls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scirii" title="Scirii">Scirii</a> among the East Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury200015_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury200015-82">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cimbrian_War">Cimbrian War</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg/290px-Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg/435px-Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg/580px-Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2571" data-file-height="2186" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cimbrians_and_Teutons_invasions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Migrations of the <a href="/wiki/Cimbri" title="Cimbri">Cimbri</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Teutons" title="Teutons">Teutons</a> (late 2nd century BCE) and their <a href="/wiki/Cimbrian_War" title="Cimbrian War">war with Rome</a> (113–101 BCE)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cimbrian_War" title="Cimbrian War">Cimbrian War</a></div>
<p>Late in the 2nd century BCE, Roman sources recount the migrations of the Cimbri, Teutones and <a href="/wiki/Ambrones" title="Ambrones">Ambrones</a> into <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>. This cultural confrontation resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Cimbrian_War" title="Cimbrian War">Cimbrian War</a> between the Roman Republic and the Germanic tribes; particularly those of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Consul" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Consul">Roman Consul</a> under <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Cimbri crossed into <a href="/wiki/Noricum" title="Noricum">Noricum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>) in 113 BCE looking for food and usable land when they confronted and defeated a Roman army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Noreia" title="Battle of Noreia">Battle of Noreia</a>. A combined force of Cimbri<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup> and Teutoni squared off against additional armies from Rome in 107 (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Burdigala" title="Battle of Burdigala">Battle of Burdigala</a>, 105 (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arausio" title="Battle of Arausio">Battle of Arausio</a>) and 102 BCE (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tridentum" title="Battle of Tridentum">Battle of Tridentum</a>), vanquishing them in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOzment200558fn_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOzment200558fn-84">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Their further incursions into Roman Italy were thrust back by the Romans at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aquae_Sextiae" title="Battle of Aquae Sextiae">Battle of Aquae Sextiae</a> in 102 BCE, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vercellae" title="Battle of Vercellae">Battle of Vercellae</a> in 101 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoolf2012105–107_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoolf2012105–107-85">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encounter_with_Julius_Caesar">Encounter with Julius Caesar</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a></div>
<p>These earlier invasions were written up by Caesar and others as presaging of a Northern danger for the Roman Republic, a danger that should be controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011369–371_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011369–371-86">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Julius Caesar describes the <i>Germani</i> and their customs in his <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i>, though in certain cases it is still a matter of debate if he refers to Northern <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> tribes or clearly identified Germanic tribes. Caesar notes that the Gauls had earlier dominated and sent colonies into the lands of the Germans, but that the Gauls had since degenerated under the influence of Roman civilization, and now considered themselves inferior in military prowess.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Osterby_Man.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Osterby_Man.jpg/220px-Osterby_Man.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Osterby_Man.jpg/330px-Osterby_Man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Osterby_Man.jpg/440px-Osterby_Man.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Osterby_Man.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Osterby_Man" title="Osterby Man">Osterby Head</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bog_body" title="Bog body">bog body</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Suebian_knot" title="Suebian knot">Suebian knot</a></div></div></div>
<blockquote><p>[The Germani] have neither <a href="/wiki/Druids" class="mw-redirect" title="Druids">Druids</a> to preside over sacred offices, nor do they pay great regard to sacrifices. They rank in the number of the gods those alone whom they behold, and by whose instrumentality they are obviously benefited, namely, the sun, fire, and the moon; they have not heard of the other deities even by report. Their whole life is occupied in hunting and in the pursuits of the military art; from childhood they devote themselves to fatigue and hardships. Those who have remained <a href="/wiki/Chaste" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaste">chaste</a> for the longest time, receive the greatest commendation among their people; they think that by this the growth is promoted, by this the physical powers are increased and the sinews are strengthened. And to have had knowledge of a woman before the twentieth year they reckon among the most disgraceful acts; of which matter there is no concealment, because they bathe promiscuously in the rivers and [only] use skins or small cloaks of deer's hides, a large portion of the body being in consequence naked.</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/330px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/495px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg/660px-Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="1181" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cesare_prima_Gallia_58_a.C.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman world in 58 BC before the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a>. Germanic territories are shown in pink.</div></div></div>
<blockquote><p>They do not pay much attention to agriculture, and a large portion of their food consists in milk, cheese, and flesh; nor has any one a fixed quantity of land or his own individual limits; but the magistrates and the leading men each year apportion to the tribes and families, who have united together, as much land as, and in the place in which, they think proper, and the year after compel them to remove elsewhere. For this enactment they advance many reasons-lest seduced by long-continued custom, they may exchange their ardor in the waging of war for agriculture; lest they may be anxious to acquire extensive estates, and the more powerful drive the weaker from their possessions; lest they construct their houses with too great a desire to avoid cold and heat; lest the desire of wealth spring up, from which cause divisions and discords arise; and that they may keep the common people in a contented state of mind, when each sees his own means placed on an equality with [those of] the most powerful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar2019153–154,_6.20–6.21_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar2019153–154,_6.20–6.21-91">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Tacitus described the Germanic people as ethnically uniform or "unmixed" with "a distinct character" and he even generalized them by claiming that "a family likeness pervades the whole." He also reported that the peoples of Germany have fierce <a href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_hair" title="Red hair">red hair</a>, and large bodies" that rendered them capable of "violent" outbursts, unable to tolerate heat or thirst but well accustomed to the cold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200939_&#91;Ch._4&#93;_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200939_[Ch._4]-93">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> This image portrayed them as a fearsome people deserving Rome's attention. Caesar was wary of these barbaric people of <i>Germania</i> and invoked the threat of expansions such as that by <a href="/wiki/Ariovistus" title="Ariovistus">Ariovistus</a>' Suebi as justification for his brutal campaigns to annex Gaul to Rome in 58–51 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagden200122_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagden200122-94">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/330px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/495px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/660px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="1181" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman world during the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Augustus" title="Wars of Augustus">Wars of Augustus</a>, showing Roman annexations in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>An intense Roman militarization, greater than ever before, was begun under Caesar to deal with the barbarian tribes along the frontier — particularly since he feared that the Celtic Gauls between Rome and the Germanic people would not be able to defend themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006302_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006302-95">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> One major Celtic people who were forced from their homeland in modern southwest Germany and Bohemia were the <a href="/wiki/Boii" title="Boii">Boii</a>, a migration which had major impacts on Rome and many other peoples. Later, Caesar's attention in 58 BCE was drawn to the movements of the Boii's old neighbours the <a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a>, another population group forced into Gaul from the direction of modern southwest Germany and western <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199923_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199923-96">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;p&#93;</a></sup> When the Gaulish <a href="/wiki/Arverni" title="Arverni">Arverni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sequani" title="Sequani">Sequani</a> elicited assistance from the Germanic Suebi (who came to them from east of the Rhine into Gaul) against their <a href="/wiki/Aedui" title="Aedui">Aedui</a> enemies in 71 BCE, the Suebi essentially remained <i>in situ</i> and were able to expand further into the territory along the periphery of the Roman frontier. Meanwhile, Celtic culture and influence in Gaul began to wane during the first century BCE as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199923–24_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199923–24-98">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roman expansion along the Rhine and Danube rivers resulted in the incorporation of many indigenous Celtic societies into the Roman Empire. Lands to the north and east of the Rhine emerge in the Roman records under the name <i><a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a></i>. Population groups from this area had a complex relationship with Rome; sometimes the peoples of <i>Germania</i> were at war with Rome, but at times they established trade relations, symbiotic military alliances, and cultural exchanges with one another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006301–302_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006301–302-99">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the Romans made concerted efforts to divide the Germanic tribes when the opportunity presented itself, encouraging intertribal rivalry so as to diminish the threat of an otherwise formidable enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOzment200519_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOzment200519-100">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Over the following centuries, the Romans sometimes intervened, but often took advantage as their neighbors slaughtered one another using Roman-influenced techniques of war. More instances of <i>Germani</i> fighting <i>Germani</i> appear in the works of Tacitus than between Romans and <i>Germani</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPohl200216_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPohl200216-101">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> But it was Caesar's wars against the Germanic people that helped establish and solidify the use of the term <i>Germania</i>. The initial purpose of the Roman military campaigns was to protect Trans-Alpine Gaul from further incursions of the Germanic tribes by controlling the area between the Rhine and the Elbe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram199736–37_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram199736–37-102">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Roman_Empire_period">Early Roman Empire period</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Roman_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Iron Age">Roman Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Imperial_campaigns_in_Germania" title="Early Imperial campaigns in Germania">Early Imperial campaigns in Germania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arminius_pushkin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Arminius_pushkin.jpg/200px-Arminius_pushkin.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Arminius_pushkin.jpg/300px-Arminius_pushkin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Arminius_pushkin.jpg/400px-Arminius_pushkin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1074" data-file-height="1370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arminius_pushkin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman sculpture of a young man sometimes identified as <a href="/wiki/Arminius" title="Arminius">Arminius</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustean</a> period there was—as a result of Roman activity as far as the Elbe River—a first definition of the "Germania magna" from the Rhine and Danube rivers in the West and South to the Vistula and the Baltic Sea in the East and North. In 9 CE, a revolt of their Germanic subjects headed by <a href="/wiki/Arminius" title="Arminius">Arminius</a> resulted in a decisive defeat of <a href="/wiki/Publius_Quinctilius_Varus" title="Publius Quinctilius Varus">Publius Quinctilius Varus</a> and the destruction of three Roman legions in a surprise attack at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Teutoburg_Forest" title="Battle of the Teutoburg Forest">Battle of the Teutoburg Forest</a>), which caused withdrawal of the Roman frontier to the Rhine. Occupying Germany had proven costly and Arminius' attack helped bring about the end of 28 years of Roman campaigning across the North European plains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011384_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011384-103">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Both Arminius and another contemporary Germanic warrior king named <a href="/wiki/Maroboduus" title="Maroboduus">Maroboduus</a>, attempted to rule these warrior-based peoples in autocratic fashion but were deposed or outright killed through the treachery of other warrior-nobles, who strove for their own glory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199933_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199933-104">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>During the reign of Augustus, Germanic warriors, particularly men of the <a href="/wiki/Batavi_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Batavi (Germanic tribe)">Batavi</a>, were recruited as personal bodyguards to the Roman emperor, forming the so-called <a href="/wiki/Numerus_Batavorum" title="Numerus Batavorum">Numerus Batavorum</a>. In 69 AD, the turbulent <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Civilis" title="Gaius Julius Civilis">Gaius Julius Civilis</a>, a Roman military officer of Batavian origin, orchestrated the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Batavi" title="Revolt of the Batavi">Revolt of the Batavi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2016201,_210,_212_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2016201,_210,_212-105">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The revolt lasted nearly a year and while it was ultimately unsuccessful,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2016210_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2016210-106">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Civilis managed to evade Roman capture.
</p>
<blockquote><p>"Let Syria, Asia Minor, and the East, habituated as it is to despotism, submit to slavery... Freedom is a gift bestowed by nature even on the dumb animals. Courage is the peculiar excellence of man, and the Gods help the braver side."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus1873150_&#91;Book_4,_Ch._17&#93;_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus1873150_[Book_4,_Ch._17]-107">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
- <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Civilis" title="Gaius Julius Civilis">Gaius Julius Civilis</a></p></blockquote>
<p>At the end of the 1st century, two provinces west of the Rhine called <a href="/wiki/Germania_inferior" class="mw-redirect" title="Germania inferior">Germania inferior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germania_superior" class="mw-redirect" title="Germania superior">Germania superior</a> were established by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a>, having previously been military districts, "so as to separate this more militarized zone from the civilian populations farther west and south".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoatwrightGargolaTalbert2004360_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoatwrightGargolaTalbert2004360-108">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Important medieval cities like <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speyer" title="Speyer">Speyer</a> were part of these two "militarized" Roman provinces.
</p>
<blockquote><p>"Neither the <a href="/wiki/Samnites" title="Samnites">Samnites</a> nor the <a href="/wiki/Carthaginians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carthaginians">Carthaginians</a> nor Spain nor <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> nor even the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> have taught us more frequent lessons. The freedom of the Germans does indeed show more aggression than the despotism of the <a href="/wiki/Arsacids" class="mw-redirect" title="Arsacids">Arsacids</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200937_&#91;Ch._56&#93;_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200937_[Ch._56]-109">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
- <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a></p></blockquote>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i> by Gaius Cornelius Tacitus, an <a href="/wiki/Ethnographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnographic">ethnographic</a> work on the diverse group of Germanic tribes outside of the Roman Empire, is our most important source on the Germanic peoples of the 1st century. Germanic expansions during early Roman times are known only generally, but it is clear that the forebears of the Goths were settled on the southern Baltic shore by 100 CE. According to historian Thomas Burns, major hostilities between the external Germanic peoples of the north and Rome did not commence in "earnest" until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (CE 98—117), who used the "full weight of Roman might" to attack the Dacians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2003183_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2003183-110">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">There is not upon the Face of the Earth, a bolder, or a more indefatigable Nation than the Germans... [Y]et upon encounter, they are broken and destroyed through their own undiciplined temerity, even by the most effiminate of men<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeneca1776218_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeneca1776218-111">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>In the absence of large-scale political unification, such as that imposed forcibly by the Romans upon the peoples of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the various tribes remained free, led by their own hereditary or chosen leaders. Once Rome faced significant threats on its borders, some of the Germanic tribes who once guarded its periphery chose solace within the Roman empire itself, implying that enough assimilation and cross-cultural pollination had occurred for their societies not only to cooperate, but to live together in some cases. The 4th century Gothic <a href="/wiki/Thervingi" title="Thervingi">Thervingi</a> are most famous among scholars of classical Rome and pre-modern Europe because the majority of them sought asylum inside the heart of the Roman Empire in 376 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather2012594_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather2012594-112">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;q&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_and_co-existence_with_the_Roman_Empire">Conflict and co-existence with the Roman Empire</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Numerus_Batavorum" title="Numerus Batavorum">Numerus Batavorum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_125_de.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Roman_Empire_125_de.svg/400px-Roman_Empire_125_de.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="317" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Roman_Empire_125_de.svg/600px-Roman_Empire_125_de.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Roman_Empire_125_de.svg/800px-Roman_Empire_125_de.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2380" data-file-height="1884" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_125_de.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of Germanic, <a href="/wiki/Vistula_Veneti" title="Vistula Veneti">Venedi</a> (Slavic), and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatian</a> (Iranian) tribes on the frontier of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, 125 AD</div></div></div>
<p>By the middle to late second century CE, migrating Germanic tribes like the Marcomanni and <a href="/wiki/Quadi" title="Quadi">Quadi</a> pushed their way to the Roman frontier along the Danube corridor, movements of people which resulted in conflicts known as the <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a>; these conflicts ended in approximately CE 180.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006304_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006304-115">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Not long thereafter, larger confederations of Germanic people appeared, groups led by tribal leaders acting as would-be kings. The first of these conglomerations mentioned in the historical sources were the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a> (a term meaning "all men") who appear in Roman texts sometime in the 3rd century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999109_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999109-116">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> This change indicated that the tribalism of the Germanic people was being abandoned for consolidated rule. Meanwhile, Rome adapted itself due to the arrival of the Germanic tribes. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Severus_Alexander" title="Severus Alexander">Severus Alexander</a> was killed by his own soldiers in CE 235 for example (for negotiating peace with the tribes of <i>Germania</i> through diplomacy and bribery against the wishes of his men) and the general <a href="/wiki/Maximinus_Thrax" title="Maximinus Thrax">Maximin</a> elected in his place. Maximin was himself not Roman but was ethnically the child of a Germanic Alan and a Goth. Military expediency trumped aristocratic privilege when it came to securing the Empire and a series of professional military emperors followed as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins19992–3_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins19992–3-117">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first recorded great migration of a Germanic tribe occurred sometime at the end of the 2nd century when the Goths left the lower Vistula for the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury200016_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury200016-118">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> For the next couple hundred years, the restless Goths were a menace to the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury200016–33_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury200016–33-119">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Between the 2nd and 4th centuries the Goths slowly filtered deeper into the south and eastwards, making their way to what is now <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> in Ukraine and pressuring Rome in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyGearyO&#39;Brien2008166_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyGearyO&#39;Brien2008166-120">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Around CE 238, the Goths make their first clear impact on Roman history, having moved from the Baltic sea to the area of the modern Ukraine. Sometime in CE 250, the Gothic king <a href="/wiki/Cniva" title="Cniva">Cniva</a> employed the assistance of the Bastarnae, Carpi, various Goths, and the <a href="/wiki/Taifali" class="mw-redirect" title="Taifali">Taifali</a> when he eventually laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Philippopolis_(Thrace)" title="Philippopolis (Thrace)">Philippopolis</a>; he followed this victory up with another on the marshy terrain at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Abritus" title="Battle of Abritus">Abrittus</a>, a battle which cost the life of Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Decius" title="Decius">Decius</a> and inaugurated a series of consecutive barbarian invasions of the northern Balkans and <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd1999150_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd1999150-121">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Close to the same time that the Goths were fighting the Romans in the Balkans, there is also the first mention of the Franks around CE 250.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006304–305_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006304–305-122">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Perennial internal conflicts among several successive emperors of both the eastern and western Empire during the 4th century CE resulted in civil wars and damaged the overall quality of the Roman army; the fighting also depleted the elite from within their officer corps. To compensate for their losses the Romans recruited inferior untried Roman civilians and sought replacements from across the frontier region by militarily proficient barbarian troops, a development which further strengthened the position of the Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins199946_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins199946-123">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Attempting to control the periphery of the Roman empire meant finding innovative ways of dealing with the Germanic people, so the Romans enlisted them as <i><a href="/wiki/Foederati" title="Foederati">foederati</a></i> (federates) and by the late fourth century, the majority of the Roman military was made up of Germanic warriors. Federating whole tribes of Germanic people into the Empire marked a whole new phase of encroachment and facilitated the fragmentation of Rome from within its own borders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury200061_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury200061-124">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In 260 AD, as the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a> reached its climax, <a href="/wiki/Postumus" title="Postumus">Postumus</a>, a Germanic soldier in Roman service, established the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Empire" title="Gallic Empire">Gallic Empire</a>, which claimed suzerainty over Germania, Gaul, Hispania and Britannia. Postumus was eventually assassinated by his own followers, after which the Gallic Empire quickly disintegrated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram199746–49_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram199746–49-125">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg/400px-Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg/600px-Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg/800px-Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5120" data-file-height="3938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gothic_raids_in_the_3rd_century.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Gothic</a> invasions of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the 3rd century</div></div></div>
<p>Among the Romans, the Germanic presence in the military was so extensive for example, that the word <i>barbarus</i> became a synonym for "soldier" and the imperial budget of the military was known as the <i>ficus barbarus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;r&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;s&#93;</a></sup> Barbarians (Germanics) composed the mobile army of emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine</a> with many of them, particularly the more organized ones like the Franks and Alamanni, reaching levels of high command. Constantine credited the military victories which enabled his rise to power to his Germanic troops, and is said to have recruited 40,000 Goths alone, who were tasked with guarding <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. By this time, conventional Roman troops where rapidly losing military value.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;t&#93;</a></sup> Despite Germanic peoples in many cases being enemies of the Romans, Germanic warriors in Roman service enabled the Roman Empire to survive longer than it would under other circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;u&#93;</a></sup> Earlier accounts from Julius Caesar and Tacitus suggest ancient Germanic warriors considered themselves superior to the Romans. Suebian king Ariovistus and the <a href="/wiki/Frisii" title="Frisii">Frisian</a> kings <a href="/wiki/Malorix_and_Verritus" title="Malorix and Verritus">Malorix and Verritus</a> are recorded by Roman historians boasting of supposed Germanic military superiority.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;v&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;w&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An example of Germanic prominence in the Roman army shows in the fact that in CE 350 the Frankish general <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Silvanus" title="Claudius Silvanus">Claudius Silvanus</a> was the high military commander of Gaul.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006306_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006306-136">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Warriors and leaders among the Germanic peoples had an advantage over their Roman counterparts as they knew and could dexterously traverse both worlds, whereas the Romans despised barbarian culture and customs and were unable to secure trust amid the Germanic soldiers on their payrolls. In this way, the ethnic and regional ties within the evolving bureaucratic Roman-Germanic world began to favor the barbarians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPohl199734–35_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPohl199734–35-137">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Roman_Britannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Britannia">Roman Britannia</a> was contemporaneously under constant threat during the 3rd and 4th centuries CE by northern <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> as well as the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> who sailed from north of Gaul to the eastern coast of the British Isles. Late in CE 367, the Roman garrisons in Britannia collapsed as the Germanic barbarians poured into the region from all directions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer201045_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer201045-138">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Attempting to permanently reestablish control on Britannia, the emperor <a href="/wiki/Valentinian_I" title="Valentinian I">Valentinian I</a> sent an experienced Roman commander who was able to beat the invaders back after a year-long war and gain control of Londonium, but it was a Pyrrhic victory, for the Germanic invaders had burned down standing settlements, ravaged cities on the isles, interrupted trade and annihilated entire Roman garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer201045–46_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer201045–46-139">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> By the middle of the 5th century, the Picts, Scots and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> began to dominate the once Roman Britannia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury2000129–130_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury2000129–130-140">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_400ad_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg/400px-East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg/600px-East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg/800px-East-Hem_400ad_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1079" data-file-height="574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_400ad_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe in 400 AD on the eve of the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Hunnic</a> invasions. Germanic tribes are marked in blue text.</div></div></div>
<p>During the fourth and fifth centuries CE Roman emperors did their best to stave off the advance of the Germanic tribes. While the rulers in the Eastern Empire were able to endure the frequent clashes without serious consequences to their territorial dominion, this was not the case in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. For upwards of two centuries, the Roman emperors fought and confined the Germanic tribes to Rhine-Danube frontier and in far-away Britain, but all that changed in CE 378 when the Visigoths destroyed as much as two-thirds of the Roman army of the East under emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz195588_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz195588-141">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a> referred to the damage inflicted by the Germanic tribes at Adrianople as an "irreparable disaster" and ended his account of Roman history with this battle. Subsequent historians like Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> (among others) ascribe a similar significance to this event and call the Battle of Adrianople a watershed moment between the ancient world and the medieval one that followed; for not only did this battle reveal Rome's weakness to the Germanic tribes and inspire them accordingly, never again were they to leave Roman soil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz195588–89_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz195588–89-142">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of the trauma suffered at the hands of the ransacking Visigoths shows up in the writings of the former bishop of <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a>, who wrote about melting down golden church plates early in his episcopate so as to help the victims of the calamity at Adrianople.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2012128_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2012128-143">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_Period">Migration Period</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crossing_of_the_Rhine" title="Crossing of the Rhine">Crossing of the Rhine</a></div>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The Germans, our ferocious and implacable foe<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams1998_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams1998-144">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The arrival of the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> along the Black Sea corridor in CE 375 further caused a Germanic exodus across the Roman border.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManco2013204_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManco2013204-145">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Germanic people from the northern coasts of Europe had been making their way into Britain for several centuries before the larger-scale incursions took place.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200626_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200626-146">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Some Germanic tribes, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Gepids" title="Gepids">Gepids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, joined the Huns, and played a prominent role in the Hunnic Empire, where Gothic became the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;x&#93;</a></sup> These Germanic tribes fought with <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a> against the Western Roman Empire and other Germanic tribes at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Catalaunian_Plains" title="Battle of the Catalaunian Plains">Battle of the Catalaunian Plains</a>, in which Attila was defeated. After the death of Attila soon afterwards, a coalition of Germanic tribes led by the Gepid king <a href="/wiki/Ardaric" title="Ardaric">Ardaric</a> broke loose from Hunnic control at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nedao" title="Battle of Nedao">Battle of Nedao</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/330px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/495px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png/660px-Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png 2x" data-file-width="1954" data-file-height="1362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Invasions_of_the_Roman_Empire_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2nd century to 6th century simplified migrations</div></div></div>
<p>By the 5th century CE, the Western Roman Empire was losing military strength and political cohesion; numerous Germanic peoples, under pressure from population growth and invading Asian groups, began migrating en masse in far and diverse directions, taking them to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and far south through present day <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Over time, this wandering meant intrusions into other tribal territories, and the ensuing wars for land escalated with the dwindling amount of unoccupied territory. Roaming tribes of Germanic people then began staking out permanent homes as a means of protection. Much of this resulted in fixed settlements from which many, under a powerful leader, expanded outwards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames199560–67_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames199560–67-149">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, Visigoths, and Lombards made their way into Italy; Vandals, Burgundians, Franks, and Visigoths conquered much of Gaul; Vandals and Visigoths also pushed into Spain; Vandals additionally made it into North Africa; the Alamanni established a strong presence in the middle Rhine and Alps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200781_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater200781-150">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark the <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> merged with the Danes, in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> the <a href="/wiki/Geats" title="Geats">Geats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gutes" title="Gutes">Gutes</a> merged with the <a href="/wiki/Swedes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Swedes (Germanic tribe)">Swedes</a>. In England, the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> merged with the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a> and other groups (notably the <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a>), as well as absorbing some natives, to form the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> (later known as the English).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKendrick201360–63_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKendrick201360–63-151">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Essentially  Roman civilization was overrun by these variants of Germanic peoples during the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagden200137_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagden200137-152">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A direct result of the Roman retreat was the disappearance of imported products like ceramics and coins, and a return to virtually unchanged local Iron Age production methods. According to recent views this has caused confusion for decades, and theories assuming the total abandonment of the coastal regions to account for an archaeological time gap that never existed have been renounced. Instead, it has been confirmed that the Frisian graves had been used without interruption between the 4th and 9th centuries and that inhabited areas show continuity with the Roman period in revealing coins, jewellery and ceramics of the 5th century. Also, people continued to live in the same three-aisled farmhouse, while to the east completely new types of buildings arose. More to the south in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, archaeological evidence from this period indicates immigration from the north.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloemersvan_Dorp1991329–338_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloemersvan_Dorp1991329–338-153">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of the Western Roman Empire">Decline of the Western Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">Barbarian kingdoms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/260px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/390px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/520px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png 2x" data-file-width="2830" data-file-height="1967" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germanic</a> kingdoms and tribes after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> in 476 CE</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the Germanic tribes are frequently credited in popular depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Roman Empire</a> in the 5th century. Many <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeologists</a> have since the 1950s shifted their interpretations in such a way that the Germanic peoples are no longer seen as <i>invading</i> a decaying empire but as being <i>co-opted</i> into helping defend territory the central government could no longer adequately administer.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;y&#93;</a></sup> Germanic tribes nonetheless fought against Roman dominance when necessary. When the Roman Empire refused to allow the Visigoths to settle in Noricum for instance, they responded by <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">sacking Rome in CE 410</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Alaric_I" title="Alaric I">Alaric I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1998229_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1998229-155">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Oddly enough, Alaric I did not see his imposition in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> as an attack against the Roman Empire per se but as an attempt to gain a favorable position within its borders, particularly since the Visigoths held the Empire in high regard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury200065–66_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury200065–66-156">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alaric certainly had no intentions to destroy the great city which was symbolic of Roman power, but he needed to pay his army and the spoils of the city not only afforded the ability to do that, its wealth made him "the richest general in the empire."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2012294_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2012294-157">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> For the next year, Alaric extracted vast sums from the city; this included 5,000 pounds of gold, 30,000 pounds of silver, 5,000 pounds of oriental pepper, gilded statues from the Forum, and even the one-ton solid silver dome which Constantine once placed over the baptismal basin next to the <a href="/wiki/Archbasilica_of_Saint_John_Lateran" title="Archbasilica of Saint John Lateran">Lateran basilica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown2012294–295_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown2012294–295-158">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Not only was Alaric able to bleed Rome, he also established a Gothic confederation consisting of Theruingian and <a href="/wiki/Greuthungi" title="Greuthungi">Greuthungic</a> peoples, and he played the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman Empire">eastern</a> and western Roman Empires off against one another for his benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins199953–54_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins199953–54-159">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At about the same time Alaric was sacking the Empire's capital, there was a Roman exodus from the British Isles, a departure which provided the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> and Saxons the opportunity to occupy and control the eastern coastlands of Britain, the southern regions of Sussex, and move into the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames">Thames</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1998231–232_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1998231–232-160">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> While Germanic tribes overran the once western Roman provinces, they also continued to strive for regional ascendancy closer to Rome's center; meanwhile the threat along the periphery from the Huns created additional difficulties for the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1998232_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1998232-161">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Sometime during the 4th or 5th century CE, the Bastarnae were defeated by the Huns, ending their regional domination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather2005154_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather2005154-162">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason200661-75">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Odovacar_Ravenna_477.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Odovacar_Ravenna_477.jpg/260px-Odovacar_Ravenna_477.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Odovacar_Ravenna_477.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="280" data-file-height="298" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Odovacar_Ravenna_477.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a>, Ravenna, 477, with Odoacer in profile, depicted with a "barbarian" <a href="/wiki/Moustache" title="Moustache">moustache</a>.</div></div></div><p>
Individuals and small groups from Germanic tribes had long been recruited from the territories beyond the <i><a href="/wiki/Limes" title="Limes">limes</a></i> (i.e., the regions just outside the Roman Empire), and some of them had risen high in the command structure of the army. The Rhine and Danube provided the bulk of geographic separation for the Roman <i>limes</i>. On one side of the <i>limes</i> stood 'Latin' Europe, law, Roman order, prosperous trading markets, towns and everything that constituted modern civilization for that era; while on the other side stood barbarism, technical backwardness, illiteracy and a tribal society of fierce warriors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts1997146–147_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts1997146–147-163">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Then the Empire recruited entire tribal groups under their native leaders as military officers. Historian Evangelos Chrysos argues the implications concerning the recruitment of the barbarians into the Roman army during the migration period were enormous and relates that:</p><blockquote><p>it offered them experience of how the imperial army was organized, how the government arranged the military and functional logistics of their involvement as soldiers or officers and how it administered their practical life, how the professional expertise and the social values of the individual soldier were cultivated in the camp and on the battlefield, how the ideas about the state and its objectives were to be implemented by men in uniform, how the Empire was composed and how it functioned at an administrative level. This knowledge of and experience with the Romans opened to individual members of the <i>gentes</i> a path which, once taken, would lead them to more or less substantial affiliation or even solidarity with the Roman world. To take an example from the economic sphere: The service in the Roman army introduced the individual or corporate members into the monetary system of the Empire since quite a substantial part of their salary was paid to them in cash. With money in their hands the "guests" were by necessity exposed to the possibility of taking part in the economic system, of becoming accustomed to the rules of the wide market, of absorbing the messages of or reacting to the imperial propaganda passed to the citizens through the legends on the coins. In addition the goods offered in the markets influenced and transformed the newcomers' food and aesthetic tastes and their cultural horizon. Furthermore Roman <i>civilitas</i> was an attractive goal for every individual wishing to succeed in his social advancement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrysos200313–14_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrysos200313–14-164">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> Assisting with defense eventually shifted into administration and then outright rule, as Roman government passed into the hands of Germanic leaders. <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a> (who commanded the German mercenaries in Italy)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006307_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006307-165">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> deposed <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustulus" title="Romulus Augustulus">Romulus Augustulus</a>, the last emperor of the West in CE 476.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200564_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200564-166">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Odoacer ruled from Rome and <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, restored the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a> and assigned seats to senatorial dignitaries as part of the process of consolidating his rule.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Donnell2008105_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Donnell2008105-167">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The presence of <a href="/wiki/Successor_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Successor state">successor states</a> controlled by a <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> from one of the Germanic tribes is evident in the 6th century – even in Italy, the former heart of the Empire, where <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a> was followed by <a href="/wiki/Theodoric_the_Great" title="Theodoric the Great">Theodoric the Great</a>, king of the Ostrogoths, who was regarded by Roman citizens and Gothic settlers alike as legitimate successor to the rule of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESantosuo200413–15_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESantosuo200413–15-168">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png/260px-Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png/390px-Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png/520px-Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png 2x" data-file-width="822" data-file-height="661" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanic_kingdoms_526CE.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Germanic kingdoms in 526 CE</div></div></div>
<p>Theodoric ruled from CE 493–526, twice as long as his predecessor, and his rule is evidenced by an abundance of documents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Donnell2008105–107_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Donnell2008105–107-169">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Under the Ostrogoths a considerable degree of Roman and Germanic cultural and political fusion was achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006308_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006308-170">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Germanic kings worked in-tandem with Roman administrators to the extent possible to help ensure a smooth transition and to facilitate the profitable administration of once Roman lands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200569–70_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200569–70-171">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Slowly but surely, the distinction between Germanic rulers and Roman subjects faded, followed by varying degrees of "cultural assimilation" which included the adoption of the Gothic language by some of the indigenous people of the former Roman Empire but this was certainly not ubiquitous as Gothic identity still remained distinctive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200572_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200572-172">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Theodoric may have tried too hard to accommodate the various people under his dominion; indulging "Romans and Goths, <a href="/wiki/Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arians" class="mw-redirect" title="Arians">Arians</a>, Latin and barbarian culture" resulted in the eventual failure of the Ostrogothic reign and the subsequent "end of Italy as the heartland of late antiquity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram1988332_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram1988332-173">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to noted historian <a href="/wiki/Herwig_Wolfram" title="Herwig Wolfram">Herwig Wolfram</a>, the Germanic peoples did not and could not "conquer the more advanced Roman world" nor were they able to "restore it as a political and economic entity"; instead, he asserts that the empire's "universalism" was replaced by "tribal particularism" which gave way to "regional patriotism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997308_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997308-174">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, the entry of the Germanic tribes deep into the heart of Europe and the subsequent collapse of the western Roman Empire resulted in a "massive disruption" to long established communication networks, a system that had in many ways "bound much of the continent together for centuries."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011442_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011442-175">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Trade networks and routes shifted accordingly, <a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">Germanic kingdoms</a> and peoples established boundaries and it was not until the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">appearance</a> of the Arabs in Iberia and into Anatolia that Europeans began reestablishing their networks to deal with a new threat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011442–444_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe2011442–444-176">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The Germanic peoples who overran the Western Roman Empire probably numbered less than 100,000 people per tribe, including approximately 15,000-20,000 warriors. They constituted a tiny minority of the population in the lands over which they seized control.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;z&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;aa&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;ab&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among these tribes, the Ostrogoths in Italy and the Visigoths in Spain are recorded to have enacted <a href="/wiki/Anti-miscegenation_laws" title="Anti-miscegenation laws">laws against intermarriage</a> in order to preserve their identity.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;ac&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;ad&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franks_expansion.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Franks_expansion.gif/250px-Franks_expansion.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Franks_expansion.gif/375px-Franks_expansion.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Franks_expansion.gif/500px-Franks_expansion.gif 2x" data-file-width="790" data-file-height="505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franks_expansion.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frankish expansion from the early kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> (481) to the divisions of <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Charlemagne's Empire</a> (843/870)</div></div></div>
<p>The transition of the Migration period to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> proper took place over the course of the second half of the 1st millennium. It was marked by the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Germanic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianization of the Germanic peoples">Christianization of the Germanic peoples</a> and the formation of stable <a href="/wiki/Germanic_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic monarchy">kingdoms</a> replacing the mostly tribal structures of the Migration period. Some of this stability is discernible in the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> recognized Theodoric's reign when the Germanic conqueror entered Rome in CE 500, despite that Theodoric was a known practitioner of Arianism, a faith which the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> condemned in CE 325.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather201458–59_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather201458–59-185">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Theodoric's Germanic subjects and administrators from the Roman Catholic Church cooperated in serving him, helping establish a codified system of laws and ordinances which facilitated the integration of the Gothic peoples into a burgeoning empire, solidifying their place as they appropriated a Roman identity of sorts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather201461–68_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather201461–68-186">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The foundations laid by the Empire enabled the successor Germanic kingdoms to maintain a familiar structure and their success can be seen as part of the lasting triumph of Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPohl199733_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPohl199733-187">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In continental Europe, this Germanic evolution saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingian period">Merovingian period</a> under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> who had deposed the last emperor of Gaul, eclipsing lesser kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/Alemannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Alemannia">Alemannia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199619–20_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199619–20-188">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The Merovingians controlled most of Gaul under Clovis, who, through conversion to Christianity, allied himself with the Gallo-Romans. While the Merovingians were checked by the armies of the Ostrogoth Theodoric, they remained the most powerful kingdom in Western Europe and the intermixing of their people with the Romans through marriage rendered the Frankish people less a Germanic tribe and more a "European people" in a manner of speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199620_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199620-189">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Most of Gaul was under Merovingian control as was part of Italy and their overlordship extended into Germany where they reigned over the <a href="/wiki/Thuringii" title="Thuringii">Thuringians</a>, Alamans, and <a href="/wiki/Bavarii" class="mw-redirect" title="Bavarii">Bavarians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer2010172_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer2010172-190">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Evidence also exists that they may have even had suzerainty over south-east England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames199566–67_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames199566–67-191">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Frankish historian <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> relates that Clovis converted to Christianity partly as a result of his wife's urging and even more so due to having won a desperate battle after calling out to Christ. According to <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory</a>, this conversion was sincere but it also proved politically expedient as Clovis used his new faith as a means to consolidate his political power by Christianizing his army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer2010173_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer2010173-192">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;ae&#93;</a></sup> Against Germanic tradition, each of the four sons of Clovis attempted to secure power in different cities but their inability to prove themselves on the battlefield and intrigue against one another led the Visigoths back to electing their leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauer2010178–179_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauer2010178–179-194">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_kingdoms_c_800.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_kingdoms_c_800.svg/180px-British_kingdoms_c_800.svg.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_kingdoms_c_800.svg/270px-British_kingdoms_c_800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/British_kingdoms_c_800.svg/360px-British_kingdoms_c_800.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="672" data-file-height="1190" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_kingdoms_c_800.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Saxon and British kingdoms c. 800</div></div></div>
