<p>When Merovingian rule eventually weakened, they were supplanted by another powerful Frankish family, the <a href="/wiki/Carolingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingians">Carolingians</a>, a dynastic order which produced <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen199624–28_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen199624–28-195">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The coronation of Charlemagne as emperor by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> in Rome on Christmas Day, CE 800 represented a shift in the power structure from the south to the north. Frankish power ultimately laid the foundations for the modern nations of Germany and France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury2000239_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury2000239-196">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> For historians, Charlemagne's appearance in the historical chronicle of Europe also marks a transition where the voice of the north appears in its own vernacular thanks to the spread of Christianity, after which the northerners began writing in Latin, Germanic, and Celtic; whereas before, the Germanic people were only known through Roman or Greek sources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames199560_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames199560-197">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, the Germanic Anglo-Saxon tribes reigned over the south of Great Britain from approximately 519 to the tenth century until the <a href="/wiki/Wessex_hegemony" class="mw-redirect" title="Wessex hegemony">Wessex hegemony</a> became the nucleus for the unification of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan200161–65_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan200161–65-198">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts1996121–123_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts1996121–123-199">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Scandinavia was in the <a href="/wiki/Vendel_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Vendel period">Vendel period</a> and eventually entered the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Viking_expansion" title="Viking expansion">expansion</a> to <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Britain</a>, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iceland" title="History of Iceland">Iceland</a> in the west and as far as <a href="/wiki/Rus_Khaganate" class="mw-redirect" title="Rus Khaganate">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">Greece</a> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDerry201216–35_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDerry201216–35-200">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> By CE 900 the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> secured for themselves a foothold on Frankish soil along the Lower Seine River valley in what is now France that became known as <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>. Hence they became the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a>. They established the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a>, a territorial acquisition which provided them the opportunity to expand beyond Normandy into Anglo-Saxon England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006310–311_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006310–311-201">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a> which followed in CE 1066 wrought immense changes to life in England as their new Scandinavian masters altered their government, lordship, public holdings, culture and DNA pool permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESykes2006227–228,_264–266_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESykes2006227–228,_264–266-202">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 9th century AD, Swedish Vikings, known locally as the <a href="/wiki/Rus%27_people" title="Rus&#39; people">Rus'</a>, had ventured deep into Russia, where they founded the state of <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">Kievan Rus'</a>. In cooperation with <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Goths" title="Crimean Goths">Crimean Goths</a>, the Rus' destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Khazar_Khaganate" class="mw-redirect" title="Khazar Khaganate">Khazar Khaganate</a> and became the dominant power in Eastern Europe. They were eventually assimilated by the local <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">East Slavic</a> population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVasiliev1936117-135_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVasiliev1936117-135-203">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The various Germanic tribal cultures began their transformation into the larger nations of later history, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">German</a>, and in the case of <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> blending into a <a href="/wiki/Romano-Germanic_culture" title="Romano-Germanic culture">Romano-Germanic culture</a>. Many of these later nation states started originally as "client buffer states" for the Roman Empire so as to protect it from its enemies further away.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999110_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999110-204">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Eventually they carved out their own unique historical paths.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-migration_ethnogeneses">Post-migration ethnogeneses</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Romano-Germanic_culture" title="Romano-Germanic culture">Romano-Germanic culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanization_(cultural)" title="Romanization (cultural)">Romanization (cultural)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Germanic-speaking_Europe" title="Germanic-speaking Europe">Germanic-speaking Europe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_Expansion.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Viking_Expansion.svg/300px-Viking_Expansion.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Viking_Expansion.svg/450px-Viking_Expansion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Viking_Expansion.svg/600px-Viking_Expansion.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="524" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_Expansion.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing area of <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a> settlements during the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> conquests</div></div></div>
<p>The interactions of the migrating Germanic peoples and the deteriorating Roman empire formed the basis of the history and society of most of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and up to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006330-331_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006330-331-205">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Germanic tribes were no longer seen as a single ethnic grouping, and instead we can only speak of peoples who speak Germanic languages, though these are no longer mutually intelligible.
</p><p>The territory of modern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> was divided between Germanic- and Celtic-speaking groups in the last centuries BCE. The parts south of the <a href="/wiki/Limes_Germanicus" title="Limes Germanicus">Germanic</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/limes" class="extiw" title="wikt:limes"><i>limes</i></a> came under Latin influence in the early centuries CE but were swiftly conquered by Germanic groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>. The Germanic tribes of the Migration period had settled down by the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, the latest series of movements out of Scandinavia taking place during the Viking Age.
</p><p>The Goths and Vandals were linguistically assimilated to their Latin (Romance) substrate populations. Evidence exists that for 2nd- and 3rd-century Goths as well as for 4th- and 5th-century Lombards that significant population displacement throughout Roman-occupied Europe occurred. This quite likely contributed to their linguistic assimilation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather2012587–588_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather2012587–588-206">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> An exception to this pattern was the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Goths" title="Crimean Goths">Crimean Goths</a>, who preserved their dialect into the 18th century). Burgundians and Lombards were assimilated into both Latin (<a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italian</a>) and Germanic (<a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">German-speaking Swiss</a>) populations.
</p><p>The Viking Age <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse</a> people split into an <a href="/wiki/Old_East_Norse" class="mw-redirect" title="Old East Norse">Old East Norse</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Old_West_Norse" class="mw-redirect" title="Old West Norse">Old West Norse</a> group, which further separated into <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faroese_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Faroese people">Faroese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a> on one hand and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish people">Swedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danes</a> on the other. In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, there is a long history of assimilation of and by the <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami people">Sami people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Finns" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic Finns">Finnic peoples</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karelians" title="Karelians">Karelians</a>. In today's usage, the term "Nordic peoples" refers to the ethnic groups in all of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>. In Great Britain, Germanic people coalesced into the Anglo-Saxon (or <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>) people between the 8th and 10th centuries.
</p><p>The various Germanic peoples of the Migrations period eventually spread out over a vast expanse stretching from contemporary <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and from Norway to North Africa. The migrants had varying impacts in different regions. In many cases, the newcomers set themselves up as overlords of the pre-existing population. Over time, such groups underwent <a href="/wiki/Ethnogenesis" title="Ethnogenesis">ethnogenesis</a>, resulting in the creation of new cultural and ethnic identities (e.g., the Franks and <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman_culture" title="Gallo-Roman culture">Gallo-Romans</a> becoming the <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>). Thus, many of the descendants of the ancient Germanic peoples do not speak Germanic languages, as they were to a greater or lesser degree assimilated into the cosmopolitan, literate culture of the Roman world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOstler2006306–307_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOstler2006306–307-207">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Even where the descendants of Germanic peoples maintained greater continuity with their common ancestors, significant cultural and linguistic differences arose over time, as is strikingly illustrated by the different identities of Christianized Saxon subjects of the Carolingian Empire and pagan Scandinavian Vikings.
</p><p>More broadly, early Medieval Germanic peoples were often <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Assimilation (sociology)">assimilated</a> into the <i><a href="/wiki/Walha" class="mw-redirect" title="Walha">walha</a></i> substrate cultures of their subject populations. Thus, the Burgundians of <a href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a>, the Vandals of Northern Africa, and the Visigoths of France and Iberia, lost some Germanic identity and became part of <a href="/wiki/Romano-Germanic_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Romano-Germanic Europe">Romano-Germanic Europe</a>. For the Germanic Visigoths in particular, they had intimate contact with Rome for two centuries before their domination of the Iberian Peninsula and were accordingly permeated by Roman culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMenéndez-Pidal196819_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMenéndez-Pidal196819-208">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, the Franks of <a href="/wiki/Western_Francia" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Francia">Western Francia</a> form part of the ancestry of the French people.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/250px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/375px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/500px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1227" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of the Germans</a> (<i>Regnum Teutonicorum</i>) within the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, circa 1000 AD</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain</a> resulted in Anglo-Saxon (or English) displacement and cultural assimilation of the indigenous culture, the <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">Brythonic</a>-speaking British culture, causing the foundation of a new kingdom, England. As in what became England, indigenous Brythonic Celtic culture in some of the south-eastern parts of what became <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> (approximately the <a href="/wiki/Lothian_and_Borders" title="Lothian and Borders">Lothian and Borders</a> region) and areas of what became the Northwest of England (the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Rheged" title="Rheged">Rheged</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elmet" title="Elmet">Elmet</a>, etc.) succumbed to Germanic influence c.600—800, due to the extension of overlordship and settlement from the Anglo-Saxon areas to the south. Cultural and linguistic assimilation occurred less frequently between the Germanic Anglo-Saxons and the indigenous people who resided in the Roman dominated areas of England, particularly in the regions that remained previously unconquered. Anglo-Saxons occupied Somerset, the Severn valley, and Lancaster by c. 700 where they remained dominant. Over time, the Anglo-Saxons, with their distinct culture and language, displaced much of the extant Roman influence of old.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickham2009150–155_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickham2009150–155-209">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the European continent, <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a> developed into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of Germany</a>, which became the most important part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> proclaimed by <a href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto I</a> in 962 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006311-312_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006311-312-210">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boerfamily1886.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Boerfamily1886.jpg/220px-Boerfamily1886.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Boerfamily1886.jpg/330px-Boerfamily1886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Boerfamily1886.jpg/440px-Boerfamily1886.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boerfamily1886.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaners</a> are descended from 17th-century Dutch immigrants to <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a></div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps the final incursions by Germanic people which altered in some ways the ethnographic map of Europe was made by the Vikings. Between the 8th and 11th centuries, these <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a>/Norse traders and pirates ravaged most of north and central Europe as well as the British Isles, spreading eastwards as far as Russia and into Byzantium. While their initial exploits were generally raids for plunder, they later settled and mixed with the local peoples, which resulted in both conquest and colonization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClements2005214–229_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClements2005214–229-211">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Other examples of assimilation during the Viking Age include the Norse, who settled in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> along the French Atlantic coast, and the societal elite in <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus&#39;">medieval Russia</a>; among whom, many were the descendants of Slavified Norsemen (a theory contested by some Slavic scholars in the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, who named it the <i><a href="/wiki/Normanist_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Normanist theory">Normanist theory</a></i>). Known for their unique ships, there is evidence of the Viking presence all over mainland Europe, as no lands with navigable waters or coastlines escaped their pillaging. Vast territories in eastern England were overrun and occupied by the Vikings and the Danish King, <a href="/wiki/Canute" class="mw-redirect" title="Canute">Canute</a>, eventually succeeded to the English crown. <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L&#39;Anse aux Meadows">Archeological remains</a> as far as North America exist, giving evidence to the dynamism and territorial ambitions of the Norse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006310_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006310-212">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The descendants of the Germanic peoples are known as the Germanic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;af&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;ag&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;ah&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;ai&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic law">Germanic law</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_king" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic king">Germanic king</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">Thing (assembly)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mund_(law)" title="Mund (law)">Mund (law)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg/300px-Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg/450px-Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg/600px-Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1221" data-file-height="688" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Germanische-ratsversammlung_1-1250x715.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Germanic <i><a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">thing</a></i>, drawn after the depiction in a relief of the <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</a> (193&#160;<a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Common elements of Germanic society can be deduced both from <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">Roman historiography</a> and comparative evidence from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Medieval">Early Medieval</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_law,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_law,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-221">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> A main element uniting Germanic societies was <a href="/wiki/Germanic_king" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic king">kingship</a>, in origin a <a href="/wiki/Sacral_king" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacral king">sacral institution</a> combining the functions of military leader, high priest, lawmaker and judge. Germanic monarchy was <a href="/wiki/Elective_monarchy" title="Elective monarchy">elective</a>; the king was elected by the free men from among eligible candidates of a family (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">OE</a> <i>cynn</i>) tracing their ancestry to the tribe's divine or semi-divine founder.
</p><p>Caesar notes than in wartime, Germanic tribes would select a "magistrate" with supreme authority to wage war. Those who refused to follow him were considered traitors, and were subjected to social isolation:
</p>
<blockquote><p>When a nation either defends itself in war or wages it, magistrates are selected to be in charge of the war with power of life and death... Those unwilling to follow are thought as deserters and traitors and are no longer trusted in anything.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;aj&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to Tacitus, traitors were on occasion hanged in trees, while cowards were disposed of by drowning them in swamps:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"Traitors and deserters are hanged on trees. Cowards, those who will not fight, and those who have defiled their bodies, are plunged into a boggy mire, with a wicker hurdle pressed on top of them."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200943_&#91;Ch._12&#93;_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200943_[Ch._12]-224">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>To a large degree, many of the extant legal records from the Germanic tribes seem to revolve around property transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver201127_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver201127-225">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In early Germanic society, the free men of property each ruled their own <a href="/wiki/Estate_(house)" class="mw-redirect" title="Estate (house)">estate</a> and were subject to the king directly, without any intermediate hierarchy as in later <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>. Free men without landed property could <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">swear fealty</a> to a man of property who as their lord would then be responsible for their upkeep, including generous <a href="/wiki/Symbel" title="Symbel">feasts</a> and gifts. This system of sworn retainers was central to early Germanic society, and the loyalty of the retainer to his lord generally replaced his family ties.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg/225px-Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg" decoding="async" width="225" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg/338px-Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg/450px-Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="572" data-file-height="730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Olav_den_helliges_saga_CK5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eorgn%C3%BDr_the_Lawspeaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Þorgnýr the Lawspeaker">Þorgnýr the Lawspeaker</a> showing the power of his office to the King of Sweden at <a href="/wiki/Gamla_Uppsala" title="Gamla Uppsala">Gamla Uppsala</a>, 1018. The lawspeaker forced King <a href="/wiki/Olof_Sk%C3%B6tkonung" title="Olof Skötkonung">Olof Skötkonung</a> not only to accept peace with his enemy, King <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Norway" title="Olaf II of Norway">Olaf the Stout</a> of Norway, but also to give his daughter to him in marriage. Illustration by C. Krogh.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Early_Germanic_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Germanic law">Early Germanic law</a> reflects a hierarchy of worth within the society of free men, reflected in the differences in <a href="/wiki/Weregild" title="Weregild">weregild</a>. Among the Anglo-Saxons, a regular free man (a <i><a href="/wiki/Churl" title="Churl">ceorl</a></i>) had a weregild of 200 <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidi</a> or gold pieces), classified as a <i>twyhyndeman</i> "200-man" for this reason, while a nobleman commanded a fee of six times that amount (<i>twelfhyndeman</i> "1200-man"). Similarly, among the Alamanni the basic weregild for a free man was 200 shillings, and the amount could be doubled or tripled according to the man's rank. Unfree serfs did not command a weregild, and the recompense paid in the event of their death was merely for material damage, 15 shillings in the case of the Alamanni, increased to 40 or 50 if the victim had been a skilled artisan.
</p><p>The social hierarchy is not only reflected in the weregild due in the case of the violent or accidental death of a man, but also in differences in fines for lesser crimes. Thus the fines for insults, injury, burglary or damage to property differ depending on the rank of the injured party.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;ak&#93;</a></sup> They do not usually depend on the rank of the guilty party, although there are some exceptions associated with royal privilege.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;al&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Free women did not have a political station of their own but inherited the rank of their father if unmarried, or their husband if married. The weregild or recompense due for the killing or injuring of a woman is notably set at twice that of a man of the same rank in <a href="/wiki/Lex_Alamannorum" title="Lex Alamannorum">Alemannic law</a>.
</p><p>All freemen had the right to participate in general assemblies or <i><a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">things</a></i>, where disputes between freemen were addressed according to customary law. The king was bound to uphold ancestral law, but was at the same time the source for new laws for cases not addressed in previous tradition. This aspect was the reason for the creation of the various Germanic law codes by the kings following their <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Germanic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianization of the Germanic peoples">conversion to Christianity</a>: besides recording inherited tribal law, these codes have the purpose of settling the position of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">church</a> and Christian <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> within society, usually setting the weregilds of the members of the clerical hierarchy parallel to that of the existing hierarchy of nobility, with the position of an <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishop</a> mirroring that of the king.
</p><p>In the case of a suspected crime, the accused could avoid punishment by presenting a fixed number of free men (their number depending on the severity of the crime) prepared to swear an <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">oath</a> on his innocence. Failing this, he could prove his innocence in a <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_combat" title="Trial by combat">trial by combat</a>. Corporal or capital punishment for free men does not figure in the Germanic law codes, and <a href="/wiki/Banishment" class="mw-redirect" title="Banishment">banishment</a> appears to be the most severe penalty issued officially. This reflects that Germanic tribal law did not have the scope of exacting <a href="/wiki/Blood_feud" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood feud">revenge</a>, which was left to the judgement of the family of the victim, but to settle damages as fairly as possible once an involved party decided to bring a dispute before the assembly. A fascinating component of early Germanic laws were the varying distinctions concerning the physical body, as each body part had a personal injury value and corresponding legal claims for personal injury viewed matters like gender, rank and status as a secondary interest when deliberating cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOliver2011203–226_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOliver2011203–226-228">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg/300px-Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg/450px-Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg/600px-Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1224" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johannes-flintoe-egil-skallarimsson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Egill_Skallagr%C3%ADmsson" title="Egill Skallagrímsson">Egill Skallagrímsson</a> engaging in <a href="/wiki/Holmgang" title="Holmgang">holmgang</a> with Berg-Önundr, painting by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Flintoe" title="Johannes Flintoe">Johannes Flintoe</a></div></div></div>
<p>Generally speaking, Roman legal codes eventually provided the model for many Germanic laws and they were fixed in writing along with Germanic legal customs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997310_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997310-229">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Traditional Germanic society was gradually replaced by the system of <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm" title="Estates of the realm">estates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> in both the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Norman England">Anglo-Norman</a> England in the 11th to 12th centuries, to some extent under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> as an indirect result of Christianisation, but also because political structures had grown too large for the flat hierarchy of a <a href="/wiki/Tribal_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribal society">tribal society</a>. The same effect of political centralization took hold in Scandinavia slightly later, in the 12th to 13th century (<a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Sturlungs" title="Age of the Sturlungs">Age of the Sturlungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consolidation_of_Sweden" title="Consolidation of Sweden">Consolidation of Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_era_in_Norway" title="Civil war era in Norway">Civil war era in Norway</a>), by the end of the 14th century culminating in the giant <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">Kalmar Union</a>. Elements of tribal law, notably the <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_combat" title="Trial by combat">wager of battle</a>, nevertheless remained in effect throughout the Middle Ages, in the case of the Holy Roman Empire until the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chamber_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Chamber Court">Imperial Chamber Court</a> in the early <a href="/wiki/German_Renaissance" title="German Renaissance">German Renaissance</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalist</a> organization of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">cantonal</a> structures remained comparatively local, the Germanic thing survived into the 21st century in the form of the <i><a href="/wiki/Landsgemeinde" title="Landsgemeinde">Landsgemeinde</a></i>, albeit subject to federal law.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/250px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/375px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg/500px-Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2760" data-file-height="1560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 3rd-century <a href="/wiki/Great_Ludovisi_sarcophagus" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Ludovisi sarcophagus">Great Ludovisi sarcophagus</a> depicts a battle between <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Romans</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic warfare">Germanic warfare</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Wars">Germanic Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_and_Vandal_warfare" title="Gothic and Vandal warfare">Gothic and Vandal warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_warfare" title="Anglo-Saxon warfare">Anglo-Saxon warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comitatus" title="Comitatus">Comitatus</a></div>
<p>Historical records of the Germanic tribes in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> east of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and west of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> do not begin until quite late in the ancient period, so only the period after 100 BCE can be examined. What is clear is that the Germanic idea of warfare was quite different from the pitched battles fought by Rome and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. Instead the Germanic tribes focused on raids. Warfare of varying size however was a distinctive feature of barbarian culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113-230">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The purpose of these was generally not to gain territory, but rather to capture resources and secure prestige. These raids were conducted by <a href="/wiki/Irregular_military" title="Irregular military">irregular troops</a>, often formed along family or village lines, in groups of 10 to about 1,000. Leaders of unusual personal magnetism could gather more soldiers for longer periods, but there was no systematic method of gathering and training men, so the death of a charismatic leader could mean the destruction of an army. Armies also often consisted of more than 50 percent noncombatants, as displaced people would travel with large groups of soldiers, the elderly, women, and children. War leaders who were able to secure ample booty for their retainers were able to grow accordingly by attracting warrior bands from nearby villages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113-230">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg/220px-Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg/330px-Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg/440px-Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1408" data-file-height="1217" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bronspl%C3%A5t_pressbleck_%C3%96land_vendeltid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut of the image on the <a href="/wiki/Vendel_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Vendel era">Vendel era</a> helmet plate found on <a href="/wiki/%C3%96land" title="Öland">Öland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, depicting <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> followed by a <a href="/wiki/Berserker" title="Berserker">berserker</a></div></div></div>
<blockquote><p>"Germans.. are of robust bodies and fall upon their enemies as soon as ever they are attacked by them; and which way soever they go, they perform great exploits."" - <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJosephus1936589_&#91;The_Antiquities_of_the_Jews._Book_XIX._15&#93;_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJosephus1936589_[The_Antiquities_of_the_Jews._Book_XIX._15]-231">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Large bodies of troops, while figuring prominently in the history books, were the exception rather than the rule of ancient warfare. Thus a typical Germanic force might consist of 100 men with the sole goal of raiding a nearby Germanic or foreign village. Thus, most warfare was at their barbarian neighbors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113_230-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999113-230">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> According to Roman sources, when the Germanci tribes did fight pitched battles, the infantry often adopted wedge formations, each wedge being led by a clan head. Legitimacy for leaders among the Germans resided in their ability to successfully lead armies to victory. Defeat on the battlefield at the hands of the Romans or other barbarians often meant the end of a ruler and in some cases, being absorbed by "another, victorious confederation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999112_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999112-232">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Though often defeated by the Romans, the Germanic tribes were remembered in Roman records as fierce combatants, whose main downfall was that they failed to join together into a collective fighting force under a unified <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">command</a>, which allowed the Roman Empire to employ a "divide and conquer" strategy against them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArcherFerrisHerwigTravers2008105_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArcherFerrisHerwigTravers2008105-233">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> On occasions when the Germanic tribes worked together, the results were impressive. Three <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">Roman legions</a> were ambushed and destroyed by an alliance of Germanic tribes headed by Arminius at the Battle of the Teutoburg Forest in 9 CE, the Roman Empire made no further concentrated attempts at conquering Germania beyond the Rhine.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts199665–66_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts199665–66-234">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 4th and 5th centuries CE, Visigoths and Vandals militarily organized themselves to sufficiently challenge and sack Rome in CE 410 and again in CE 455. Then in CE 476, the last Roman emperor was deposed by a German chieftain, an event which effectively ended Roman predominance in western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanielsHyslop201485_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanielsHyslop201485-235">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Germanic tribes eventually overwhelmed and conquered the ancient world. That military transition was additionally spurred by the arrival of the Vikings from the 8th to 10th centuries, giving rise to modern Europe and medieval warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006836_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006836-236">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/220px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/330px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg/440px-Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gjermundbu_helmet_-_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gjermundbu_helmet" title="Gjermundbu helmet">Gjermundbu helmet</a> from 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a></div></div></div>
<p>For an analysis of Germanic tactics versus the Roman empire see: <a href="/wiki/Roman_infantry_tactics#Roman_infantry_versus_Gallic_and_the_Germanic_tribes" title="Roman infantry tactics">Roman infantry versus Gallic and the Germanic tribes</a>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weaponry">Weaponry</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period_spear" title="Migration Period spear">Migration Period spear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age_arms_and_armour" title="Viking Age arms and armour">Viking Age arms and armour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_sword" title="Viking sword">Viking sword</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atgeir" title="Atgeir">Atgeir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Viking_halberd" title="Viking halberd">Viking halberd</a></div>
<p>Weapons used by the Germanic tribes varied. Some of them used axes, throwing javelins, spears, bows and arrows along with swords. Most of the swords used by the Germanic warriors were those captured from Roman soldiers until the 4th century when German blacksmiths began making the best steel in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321-237">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Body armor was rarely worn and when it was, it was light by comparison to what the Romans employed; only war leaders wore helmets on the battlefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321–322_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321–322-128">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Commandeering of Roman weaponry was widespread and the acquisition of the superior Roman armaments allowed the Germanic leaders to exert their power in ways not previously available. It also meant fierce inter-Germanic rivalry which constituted the larger power blocks of the Germanic world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather2005458–459_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather2005458–459-238">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Much like their predecessors, the Vikings too used axes, swords, long knives, spears, oblong shields, leather or metal helmets and mail or leather coats for protection; the latter being luxuries most could not afford.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESantosuo2004143–144_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESantosuo2004143–144-239">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics">Tactics</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Svinefylking1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Svinefylking1.jpg/220px-Svinefylking1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="74" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Svinefylking1.jpg/330px-Svinefylking1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Svinefylking1.jpg/440px-Svinefylking1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="675" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Svinefylking1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sketch of the <a href="/wiki/Svinfylking" title="Svinfylking">Svinfylking</a>, a common tactic in Germanic warfare.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Furor_Teutonicus" title="Furor Teutonicus">Furor Teutonicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wagon_fort" title="Wagon fort">Wagon fort</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angrivarian_Wall" title="Angrivarian Wall">Angrivarian Wall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_raid_warfare_and_tactics" title="Viking raid warfare and tactics">Viking raid warfare and tactics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Svinfylking" title="Svinfylking">Svinfylking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berserker" title="Berserker">Berserker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shame-stroke" title="Shame-stroke">Shame-stroke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strandh%C3%B6gg" title="Strandhögg">Strandhögg</a></div>
<p>To the greatest extent, Germanic fighting units consisted of infantry who would emerge from cover and attack, but they also utilized skilled cavalrymen at times, something the Visigoths used decisively to aid in their victory at Adrianople. Cavalry warfare was limited in northern Europe due to the lack of suitably large horses for mounted troops. Caesar provided his Germanic armies with Roman mounts to enable them greater mobility and to enhance their fighting efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199936–37_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199936–37-240">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Unlike their western Celtic neighbors, the use of chariots was not picked up by the early Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199937_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199937-241">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Notwithstanding the use of an occasional fortified position, the Germanic warriors preferred to fight in the open and normally assumed the offensive rather than fight defensively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBémontMonod2012485–486_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBémontMonod2012485–486-242">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Emboldening themselves for fierce attacks, the Germanic warriors would rouse themselves to a high-pitched level of excitement and charge headlong against their enemies, which while effective for ambush operations, lacked in terms of the organizational skill needed for prolonged siege warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322-243">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The berserker mentality employed by the Germanic tribes against Rome was still in effect during the Viking era of the 8th and 9th centuries as they too believed that by summoning their gods and working themselves up, they would possess superhuman strength and be protected during battle. Such resolution led them to believe that dying in such a manner was heroic and would transport the fallen fighter straight into <a href="/wiki/Valhalla" title="Valhalla">Valhalla</a> where they would be embraced by the warrior maidens known as the Valkyries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322-243">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;am&#93;</a></sup> The later military development of armored knights and fortified castles was a response in part to the relentless plundering and raiding by the Vikings, which meant that the Germanic tribes who had settled mainland Europe and the British Isles had to adapt themselves so as to combat another Germanic tribe of interlopers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322–323_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006322–323-245">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Amber_Road" title="Amber Road">Amber Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">Thrall</a>, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lag" title="Félag">Félag</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weregild" title="Weregild">Weregild</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg/360px-Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg" decoding="async" width="360" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg/540px-Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg/720px-Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="919" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fyrkat_hus_stor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstructed Viking Age <a href="/wiki/Mead_hall" title="Mead hall">mead hall</a> (28.5 metres long).</div></div></div>
<p>Traces of the earliest pastoralism of the Germanic peoples appear in central Europe in the form of elaborate cattle burials along the Elbe and Vistula Rivers from around 4000–3000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006312_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006312-246">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> These archaeological remnants were left by the <a href="/wiki/Globular_Amphora_culture" title="Globular Amphora culture">Globular Amphora culture</a> who cleared forests for herding cattle and sometime after 3000 BCE began using wheeled carts and plows to cultivate their lands. Central to survival for their assistance in tilling the soil and supplying food, cattle became an economic resource to these early people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006313_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006313-247">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Germanic settlements were typically small, rarely containing much more than ten households, often less, and were usually located by clearings in the woods.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;an&#93;</a></sup> Settlements remained of a fairly constant size throughout the period. The buildings in these villages varied in form, but normally consisted of farmhouses surrounded by smaller buildings such as granaries and other storage rooms. The universal building material was timber. Cattle and humans usually lived together in the same house.
</p><p>Although the Germanic tribes practiced both agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Husbandry" class="mw-redirect" title="Husbandry">husbandry</a>, the latter was extremely important both as a source of dairy products and as a basis for wealth and social status, which was measured by the size of an individual's herd.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyGearyO&#39;Brien2008164_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKishlanskyGearyO&#39;Brien2008164-249">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The diet consisted mainly of the products of farming and husbandry and was supplied by hunting to a very modest extent. Barley and wheat were the most common agricultural products and were used for baking a certain flat type of bread as well as brewing beer. Evidence from a Saxon village known as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Feddersen_Wierde&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Feddersen Wierde (page does not exist)">Feddersen Wierde</a> near Cuxhaven, Germany (which existed between BCE 50 to CE 450) shows that the Germanic people cultivated oats and rye, used manure as fertilizer, and that they practiced <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsborne200839_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsborne200839-250">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nydamboat.2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nydamboat.2.jpg/220px-Nydamboat.2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nydamboat.2.jpg/330px-Nydamboat.2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nydamboat.2.jpg/440px-Nydamboat.2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nydamboat.2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron Age oak boat discovered at <a href="/wiki/Nydam_Mose" title="Nydam Mose">Nydam Mose</a> in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8nderborg" title="Sønderborg">Sønderborg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The fields were tilled with a light-weight wooden <a href="/wiki/Ard_(plough)" title="Ard (plough)">ard</a>, although heavier models also existed in some areas. Common clothing styles are known from the remarkably well-preserved <a href="/wiki/Bog_bodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Bog bodies">corpses</a> that have been found in former marshes on several locations in Denmark, and included woolen garments and brooches for women and trousers and leather caps for men. Other important small-scale industries were weaving, the manual production of basic pottery and, more rarely, the fabrication of iron tools, especially weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;_10-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;The_Imperial_Teutonic_Order&#39;&#39;-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corded_Ware_culture" title="Corded Ware culture">Corded Ware culture</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Funnelbeaker_culture" title="Funnelbeaker culture">Funnelbeaker culture</a> (circa. 2900–2300 BCE) of these north and central European peoples coincide one another and provide evidence of how they lived, traded and buried their dead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006313–314_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006313–314-251">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1300 BCE the societies of Jutland and Northern Germany along with the Celtic people experienced a major revolution in technology during the Late Bronze Age, shaping tools, containers and weapons through the improved techniques of working bronze. Both the sword and the bow and arrow as well as other weaponry proliferate and an arms race of sorts between the tribes ensued as they tried to outpace one another. Trade was taking place to a greater degree and simple gems and amber from the Mediterranean indicate that long-distance exchange of goods was occurring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006314–315_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006314–315-252">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> When the Iron Age arrived, the Germanic people showed greater mastery of ironworks than their Celtic contemporaries but they did not have the extensive trade networks during this period that their southern neighbors enjoyed with the Greco-Roman world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006315_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006315-253">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%;&#32;;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r887775652"/>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">[W]ith their great ferocity [Germans] combine great craft, to an extent scarcely credible to one who has had no experience with them, and are a race to lying born...<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler2016303_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler2016303-254">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Velleius_Paterculus" title="Marcus Velleius Paterculus">Marcus Velleius Paterculus</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The Germanic tribes appear to have been the first peoples to use the <a href="/wiki/Heavy_plough" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy plough">heavy plough</a>, which enabled them to farm the rough forested lowlands of Northern Europe. In certain their respects their technology was superior to that of the Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;ao&#93;</a></sup> They excelled particularly at <a href="/wiki/Metalwork" class="mw-redirect" title="Metalwork">metalwork</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;ap&#93;</a></sup> In many cases in fact, ancient Germanic smiths and other craftsmen produced products of higher quality than the Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;aq&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;ar&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar notes that certain warlike Germanic tribes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Nervii" title="Nervii">Nervii</a> and Suebi, practiced <a href="/wiki/Teetotalism" title="Teetotalism">teetotalism</a>, banning the import of <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> to their territories.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;as&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;at&#93;</a></sup> Early Germanic peoples are recorded as being opposed to taxation. For this reason, it is noted by <a href="/wiki/Salvian" title="Salvian">Salvian</a>, native Romans in many cases preferred "barbarian" rule over Roman rule.
</p>
<blockquote><p>"For in the Gothic country the barbarians are so far from tolerating this sort of oppression that not even Romans who live among them have to bear it. Hence all the Romans in that region have but one desire, that they may never have to return to the Roman jurisdiction. It is the unanimous prayer of the Roman people in that district that they may be permitted to continue to lead their present life among the barbarians."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKristinsson2010172_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKristinsson2010172-267">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Widening trade between the Germanic tribes and Rome started later following the Empire's wars of conquest when they looked to the Germanic people to supply them with slaves, leather and quality iron. One of the reasons the Romans may have drawn borders along the Rhine, besides the sizable population of Germanic warriors on one side of it, was that the Germanic economy was not robust enough for them to extract much booty nor were they convinced they could acquire sufficient tax revenue from any additional efforts of conquest. Drawing a distinctive line between themselves and Germanic people also incentivized alliances and trade as the Germanic people sought a share of the imperial wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManco2013202_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManco2013202-268">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Roman coinage was coveted by the Germanic people who preferred silver to gold coins, mostly likely indications that a market economy was developing. Tacitus does mention the presence of a bartering system being observable among the Germanic people, but this was not exclusive, as he also writes of their use of "gold and silver for the purpose of commerce", adding rather sardonically in his text, that they preferred silver for buying cheap everyday goods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200940_&#91;Ch._5&#93;_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200940_[Ch._5]-269">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Such observations from Tacitus aside, fine metalwork, iron and glassware was soon being traded by the Germanic peoples along the coast of the North Sea of Denmark and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006315–316_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006315–316-270">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinship_patterns">Kinship patterns</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sippe" title="Sippe">Sippe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norse_clans" title="Norse clans">Norse clans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Symbel" title="Symbel">Symbel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg/300px-Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="400" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg/450px-Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg/600px-Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1944" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drinking_scene_on_an_image_stone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A drinking scene on an <a href="/wiki/Image_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Image stone">image stone</a> from <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Museum_of_National_Antiquities" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish Museum of National Antiquities">Swedish Museum of National Antiquities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The writings of Tacitus allude to the Germanic peoples being aware of a shared ethnicity, in that, they either knew or believed that they shared a common biological ancestor with one another. Just how pervasive this awareness may have been is certainly debatable, but other factors like language, clothing, ornamentation, hair styles, weapon types, religious practices and shared oral history were likely just as significant in tribal identity for the Germanics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006318_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006318-271">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Members of a Germanic tribe told tales about the exploits of heroic founding figures who were more or less mythologized. Village life consisted of free men assembled under a chieftain, all of whom shared common cultural and political traditions. Status among the early Germanic tribes was often gauged by the size of a man's cattle herd or by one's martial prowess.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeary1999111_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeary1999111-272">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before their conversion to Christianity, the Germanic peoples of Europe were made up of several <a href="/wiki/Tribe" title="Tribe">tribes</a>, each functioning as an economic and military unit and sometimes united by a common religious cult. <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">Kinship</a>, especially close kinship, was very important to life within a tribe but generally was not the source of a tribe's identity. In fact, several elements of ancient Germanic life tended to weaken the role of kinship: the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Retinue" title="Retinue">retinues</a> surrounding military <a href="/wiki/Chieftain" class="mw-redirect" title="Chieftain">chieftains</a>, the ability of strong leaders to unite people who were not closely related, and <a href="/wiki/Feud" title="Feud">feuds</a> and other conflicts within a tribe that might lead to permanent divisions. The retinue (often called "comitatus" by scholars, following the practice of ancient Roman writers) consisted of the followers of a chieftain, who depended on the retinue for <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> and other services and who in return provided for the retinue's needs and divided with them the spoils of battle.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> This relationship between a chieftain and his followers became the basis for the more complicated <a href="/wiki/Feudal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal system">feudal system</a> that developed in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>. A chieftain's retinue might include close relatives, but it was not limited to them. Eventually the rising power of individual chieftains and kings from among the military leadership of Germanic tribes and confederations curtailed and in many ways replaced the power once enjoyed by tribal assemblies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199931–32_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199931–32-274">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> A code of ethics in battle prevailed among the Germanic kin. According to Tacitus, the greatest disgrace that can befall a warrior of a clan among the Germanic tribes was the abandonment of their shield during combat, as this almost certainly resulted in social isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200940_&#91;Ch._6&#93;_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200940_[Ch._6]-275">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Within tribal Germanic society, their social hierarchy was linked intrinsically to war and this warrior code maintained the fidelity between chiefs and their young warriors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeather2003324_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeather2003324-276">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Feuds were the standard means for resolving conflicts and regulating behavior. Peace within the tribe was about controlling violence with codes identifying exactly how certain types of feuds were to be settled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006318_271-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006318-271">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Those closely related to a person who had been injured or killed were supposed to exact revenge on or monetary payment from the offender. This duty helped reaffirm the bonds between extended family members. Yet such feuds weakened the tribe as a whole, sometimes leading to the creation of a new tribe as one group separated from the rest. Clans of Germanic people consisted of groupings of about 50 households in total with societal rules for each specific clan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317-277">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Recent scholarship suggests that, despite the obligation to take part in feuds and other customs involving kinship ties, <a href="/wiki/Extended_families" class="mw-redirect" title="Extended families">extended families</a> did not form independent units among the early Germanic peoples. Though most members of a tribe would have been more or less distantly related, common descent was not the main source of a tribe's identity, and extended families were not the main social units within a tribe. Traditional theories have emphasized the supposedly central role in Germanic culture of clans or large groups with common ancestry. But there is little evidence that such clans existed, and they were certainly not an important element of social organization. As historian Alexander C. Murray concludes, "kinship was a crucial factor in all aspects of barbarian activity, but its uses and groupings were fluid, and probably on the whole not long lasting."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray198364_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray198364-278">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Internal competition within the factions of a tribe occasionally resulted in internecine warfare which weakened and sometime destroyed a group, as appears to have been the case for the Cherusci tribe during Rome's earlier period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199930_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199930-279">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important family relationships among the early Germanic peoples were within the individual household, a fact based on the archaeological evidence from their settlements where the long-houses appeared to be central in their existence. Within the household unit, an individual was equally bound to both the mother and the father's side of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETodd199932_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETodd199932-280">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Fathers were the main figures of authority,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317_277-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317-277">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> but wives also played an important and respected role. Some Germanic tribes even believed that women possessed magical powers and were feared accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams199879_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams199879-281">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Tacitus describes how, during battles, Germanic warriors were encouraged and cared for by their wives and mothers. He also notes that during times of peace, women did most of the work of managing the household. Along with the children, they apparently did most of the household chores as well. Children were valued, and according to Tacitus, limiting or destroying one's offspring was considered shameful. Mothers apparently breast-fed their own children rather than using nurses. Besides parents and children, a household might include slaves, but slavery was uncommon, and according to Tacitus, slaves normally had households of their own. Their slaves (usually prisoners of war) were most often employed as domestic servants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317_277-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006317-277">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Polygamy and concubinage were rare but existed, at least among the upper classes.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;au&#93;</a></sup> When a certain number of families resided on the same territory, this constituted a village (<i>Dorf</i> in German). The overall territory occupied by people from the same tribe was designated in the writings of Tacitus as a <i>civitas</i>, with each of the individual <i>civitas</i> divided into <i>pagi</i> (or cantons), which were made up of several <i>vici</i>. In cases where the tribes were grouped into larger confederations or a group of kingdoms, the term <i>pagus</i> was applied (<i>Gau</i> in German).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBémontMonod2012410–415_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBémontMonod2012410–415-283">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Extensive contact with Rome altered the egalitarian structure of tribal Germanic society. As individuals rose to prominence, a distinction between commoner and nobility developed and with it the previous constructs of folkright shared equally across the tribe was replaced in some cases by privilege.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006321-237">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Germanic society became more stratified. Elites within the Germanic tribes who learned the Roman system and emulated the way they established dominion were able to gain advantages and exploit them accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPohl199734_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPohl199734-284">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Important changes began taking place by the 4th century CE as Germanic peoples, while still cognizant of their unique clan identities, started forming larger confederations of a similar culture. Gathering around the dominant tribes among them and hearkening to the most charismatic leaders brought the various barbarians tribes closer together. On the surface this change appeared to the Romans as welcome since they preferred to deal with a few strong chiefs to control the populations that they feared across the Rhine and Danube, but it eventually made these Germanic rulers of confederated peoples more and more powerful.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESantosuo20049_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESantosuo20049-285">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> While strong, they were still not federated to one another since they possessed no sense of "pan-Germanic solidarity", but this started to change noticeably by the 5th century CE at Rome's expense.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200550–51_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard-Perkins200550–51-286">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg/250px-Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg/375px-Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="508" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pietroassa_ring_1875.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Pietroassa" title="Ring of Pietroassa">Ring of Pietroassa</a> (drawing by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Trenk" class="mw-redirect" title="Henri Trenk">Henri Trenk</a>, 1875).</div></div></div>
<p>Based on the writings of Tacitus, most of the barbarians were content with one wife, which indicates a general trend towards monogamy. For those higher within their social hierarchy however, polygamy was sometimes solicited on account of their rank.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200947_&#91;Ch._18&#93;_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200947_[Ch._18]-287">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Of note, Tacitus observed that "The dowry is not brought by the wife to the husband but by the husband to the wife" and wedding gifts related to a marriage consisted of things like oxen, saddles and various armaments. Revealing the warlike nature of their society, Tacitus also reported that wives came to their husbands as a partner in toils and dangers; to suffer and to dare equally with him, in peace and in war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200947_&#91;Ch._18&#93;_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200947_[Ch._18]-287">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar notes that early Germanic peoples believed <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abstinence" title="Sexual abstinence">sexual abstinence</a> increased physical growth. For a young Germanic man, having had sexual relations with a woman before the age of twenty was considered a disgrace:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Life is all in hunting and military exercise. From childhood they train for labor and hardship. They have great praise among them for those who remain longest without sexual experience. Some think this makes for height, muscle, and strength. Indeed to have had knowledge of a woman before age twenty they think very shameful, and there is no hiding it..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar2019154,_6.21_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar2019154,_6.21-288">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Marriageable_age" title="Marriageable age">age at first marriage</a> among ancient Germanic tribes, according to <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, was late for women compared to Roman women:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The young men are slow to mate, and reach manhood with unimpaired vigour. Nor are the virgins hurried into marriage. Being as old and as tall as the men, they are equal to their mates in age and strength, and the children inherit the robustness of their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200948_&#91;Ch._20&#93;_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200948_[Ch._20]-289">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
