<p>Tacitus notes that the Germanic tribes were strictly monogameous, and that adultery was severely punished:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Considering the great size of the population, adultery is very rare. The penalty for it is instant and left to the husband. He cuts off her hair, strips her naked in the presence of kinsmen, and flogs her all through the village. They have no mercy on a woman who prostitutes her chastity. Neither beauty, nor youth, nor wealth can find her another husband. In fact, no one there laughs about vice, nor is seducing and being seduced called "modern"... To limit the number of their children or to kill one of the later-born is regarded as a crime. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETacitus200947-48_&#91;Ch._19&#93;_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETacitus200947-48_[Ch._19]-290">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>For Germanic women of later antiquity, marriage obviously had its appeal since it offered greater security and better placement in their social hierarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrassetto2003261_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrassetto2003261-291">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Where <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> had set the prime of life at 37 years for men and 18 for women, the <a href="/wiki/Visigothic_Code" title="Visigothic Code">Visigothic Code of law</a> in the 7th century placed the prime of life at twenty years for both men and women, after which both presumably married. Thus it can be presumed that ancient Germanic brides were on average about twenty and were roughly the same age as their husbands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy198573–75_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy198573–75-292">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
Tacitus, however, had never visited the German-speaking lands and most of his information on <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> comes from secondary sources. In addition, Anglo-Saxon women, like those of other Germanic tribes, are marked as women from the age of twelve onward, based on archaeological finds, implying that the age of marriage coincided with <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenSiegmund2003107_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenSiegmund2003107-293">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Generally, there were two forms of marriage among the Germanic peoples, one involving the participation of the parents and the other, those that did not. Known as <i>Friedelehe</i>, the latter form consisted of marriage between a free man and a free woman, since marriage between free persons and slaves was forbidden by law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrassetto2003262_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrassetto2003262-294">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of Germanic patriarchy is evident later in the 7th century CE <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Rothari" class="mw-redirect" title="Edict of Rothari">Edict of Rothari</a> of the Lombards, which stated that women were not allowed to live of their own freewill and that they had to be subject to a man and if no one else, they were to be "under the power of the king".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBury2000281_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBury2000281-295">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For Germanic kings, warrior chieftains, senators and Roman nobility, a certain degree of intermarriage was undertaken to strengthen their ties to one another and to the Empire, making marriage or <i>connubium</i> as the Romans connoted the bond, an instrument of politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997105_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997105-296">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Earlier treaty terms in the late 4th century CE had forbidden "foreign" Goths to intermarry with Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram199788_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram199788-297">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Some of the marriage attempts of the 6th century CE were deliberately planned for the sake of royal succession. Imperial policy had to be carefully charted between the Roman-Germanic claimants to kingship and the maintenance of Roman imperial administration, as the federated Germanic kings attempted to put their stamp on Roman rule and replace Roman armies with their own warriors. Roman leaders were not oblivious to the clever tactics (intermarriage and offspring) employed by Germanic chieftains and adopted creative treaties to either appease them or temper their ambitions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997106–107_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram1997106–107-298">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic Christianity">Germanic Christianity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg/220px-Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg/330px-Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg/440px-Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="508" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kiviksgraven_slab_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stone slab from <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_Grave" title="The King&#39;s Grave">The King's Grave</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a>, 1400 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the Middle Ages, Germanic peoples followed what is now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>: "a system of interlocking and closely interrelated religious worldviews and practices rather than as one indivisible religion" and as such consisted of "individual worshippers, family traditions and regional cults within a broadly consistent framework".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEwing20089_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEwing20089-299">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> It was <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> in nature, with some underlying similarities to other <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Indo-Germanic traditions</a>. Despite the unique practices of some tribes, there was a degree of cultural uniformity among the Germanic people concerning religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade1984154_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade1984154-300">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;av&#93;</a></sup> Germanic ideology and religious practices were pervaded and colored to a large degree by war, particularly the notion of a heroic death on the battlefield, as this brought the god(s) a "blood sacrifice."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade1984161_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade1984161-304">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;aw&#93;</a></sup> Early Germanic society was characterized by a rigorous <a href="/wiki/Code_of_ethics" class="mw-redirect" title="Code of ethics">code of ethics</a>, which above all valued trust, loyalty and courage.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;ax&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological findings suggest that the Germanic barbarians practiced some of the same 'spiritual' rituals as the Celts, including human sacrifice, divination, and the belief in spiritual connection with the natural environment around them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2003367_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2003367-307">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Germanic priestesses were feared by the Romans, as these tall women with glaring eyes, wearing flowing white gowns often wielded a knife for sacrificial offerings. Captives might have their throats cut and be bled into giant cauldrons or have their intestines opened up and the entrails thrown to the ground for prophetic readings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams199881–82_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams199881–82-308">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Spiritual rituals frequently occurred in consecrated groves or upon islands on lakes where perpetual fires burned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams199882_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams199882-309">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Germanic_deities" title="List of Germanic deities">deities found in Germanic paganism</a> appeared under similar names across the Germanic peoples, most notably the god known to the Germans as <a href="/wiki/Wodan" class="mw-redirect" title="Wodan">Wodan</a> or <a href="/wiki/W%C5%8Dden" class="mw-redirect" title="Wōden">Wotan</a>, to the Anglo-Saxons as <a href="/wiki/Woden" class="mw-redirect" title="Woden">Woden</a>, and to the Norse as <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Óðinn</a>, as well as the god <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> – known to the Germans as <a href="/wiki/Donar" class="mw-redirect" title="Donar">Donar</a>, to the Anglo-Saxons as Þunor and to the Norse as Þórr. Pagan beliefs amid the Germanic tribes were reported by some of the earlier Roman historians and in the 6th century CE another instance of this appears when the Byzantine historian and poet, <a href="/wiki/Agathias" title="Agathias">Agathias</a>, remarked that the Alamannic religion was "solidly and unsophisticatedly pagan."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater2007117_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrinkwater2007117-310">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Christianity had no relevance for the pagan barbarians until their contact and integration with Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2003368_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2003368-311">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_(KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82)_vit_bakgr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg/220px-Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg/330px-Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg/440px-Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_%28KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82%29_vit_bakgr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2363" data-file-height="2684" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Torshammare_fr_Sk%C3%A5ne_(KVHoA_Akademiens_M%C3%A5nadsblad_1895_s102_fig82)_vit_bakgr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>'s hammer in silver with filigree ornamentation was found in <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">Scania</a>. It was donated to the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_History_Museum" title="Swedish History Museum">Swedish History Museum</a> in 1895.</div></div></div>
<p>While the Germanic peoples were slowly converted to Christianity by varying means, many elements of the pre-Christian culture and indigenous beliefs remained firmly in place after the conversion process, particularly in the more rural and distant regions. Of particular note is the survival of the pagan fascination with the forest in the retention of <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">Christmas tree</a> even today. Many of the Germanic tribes actually revered forests as sacred places and left them unmolested. Conversion to Christianity broke this pagan obsession with protecting the forest in some locations and allowed once migrant tribes to settle in places where they previously refused to cultivate the soil or chop down trees based on religious belief. To that end, the Christianisation of Germanic peoples facilitated the clearing of forests and therewith provided "a broad and stable basis for the medieval economy of Central Europe" by leveraging the vast forest resources available to them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrice1965368–378_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrice1965368–378-312">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The Ostrogoths, Visigoths, and Vandals were Christianized while they were still outside the bounds of the Empire; however, they converted to Arianism rather than <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>, and were soon regarded as <a href="/wiki/Christian_heresy" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian heresy">heretics</a> by Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESantosuo200414–16_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESantosuo200414–16-313">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> The one great written remnant of the Gothic language is a <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Bible" title="Gothic Bible">translation of the Bible</a> made by Wulfila, the Arian <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> who converted them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006327_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006327-314">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Goths, Vandals, and other Germanic peoples often offered political resistance prior to their conversion to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron199797_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron199797-315">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> The Lombards were not converted until after their entrance into the Empire, but received Christianity from Arian Germanic tribes sometime during the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006497_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006497-316">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Paganism and Christianity were still being practiced across the empire when Constantine died in CE 337, despite his conversion; he did however ban pagan rituals at select religious temples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron199798_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron199798-317">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Sometime between CE 391–392, the barbarian king Theodosius I made an official proclamation which outlawed pagan religions in his region of influence with various successors like Justinian doing likewise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron199798_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron199798-317">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> were converted directly from <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a> to Catholicism under the leadership of Clovis in about CE 496 without an intervening time as Arians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_peoples,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Eventually the Gothic tribes turned away from their Arian faith and in CE 589 converted to Catholicism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPohl199737_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPohl199737-318">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Several centuries later, Anglo-Saxon and Frankish missionaries and warriors undertook the conversion of their Saxon neighbors. A key event was the felling of <a href="/wiki/Thor%27s_Oak" class="mw-redirect" title="Thor&#39;s Oak">Thor's Oak</a> near <a href="/wiki/Fritzlar" title="Fritzlar">Fritzlar</a> by <a href="/wiki/Boniface" class="mw-redirect" title="Boniface">Boniface</a>, apostle of the <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a>, in CE 723. When Thor failed to strike Boniface dead after the oak hit the ground, the Franks were amazed and began their conversion to the Christian faith.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;ay&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually for many Germanic tribes, the conversion to Christianity was achieved by armed force, successfully completed by <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, in a series of campaigns (the <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Wars" title="Saxon Wars">Saxon Wars</a>), that also brought Saxon lands into the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick2008103–106_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick2008103–106-320">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Massacres, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Verdict_of_Verden" class="mw-redirect" title="Bloody Verdict of Verden">Bloody Verdict of Verden</a>, where as many as 4,500 people were beheaded according to one of Charlemagne's chroniclers, were a direct result of this policy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson200547_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson200547-321">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Scandinavia, Germanic paganism continued to dominate until the 11th century in the form of <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_religion" title="Old Norse religion">Old Norse religion</a>, when it was gradually replaced by Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKendrick2013118–123_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKendrick2013118–123-322">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png/300px-Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png/450px-Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png/600px-Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png 2x" data-file-width="1960" data-file-height="1793" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_of_major_Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_Europe.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of major <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-DNA">Y-DNA</a> haplogroups in Europe; <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_I1" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup I1">Haplogroup I1</a> represented by light blue</div></div></div>
<p>It is suggested by geneticists that the movements of Germanic peoples has had a strong influence upon the modern distribution of the male lineage represented by the <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-DNA">Y-DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_I1_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup I1 (Y-DNA)">haplogroup I1</a>, which is believed to have originated with one man, who lived approximately 4,000 to 6,000 years ago somewhere in Northern Europe, possibly modern Denmark (see <a href="/wiki/Most_Recent_Common_Ancestor" class="mw-redirect" title="Most Recent Common Ancestor">Most Recent Common Ancestor</a> for more information). There is evidence of this man's descendants settling in all of the areas that Germanic tribes are recorded as having subsequently invaded or migrated to.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;az&#93;</a></sup> Haplogroup I1 is older than Germanic languages, but may have been present among early Germanic speakers. Other male lines likely to have been present during the development and dispersal of Germanic language populations include <a href="/wiki/R1a1a" class="mw-redirect" title="R1a1a">R1a1a</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=R1b-P312&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="R1b-P312 (page does not exist)">R1b-P312</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=R1b-U106&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="R1b-U106 (page does not exist)">R1b-U106</a>, a genetic combination of the haplogroups found to be strongly-represented among current Germanic speaking peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManco2013208_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManco2013208-324">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Peaking in northern Europe, the R1b-U106 marker seems particular interesting in distribution and provides some helpful genetic clues regarding the historical trek made by the Germanic people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManco2013209–210_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManco2013209–210-325">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_I1" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup I1">Haplogroup I1</a> accounts for approximately 40% of <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelandic</a> males, 40%–50% of <a href="/wiki/Swedes" title="Swedes">Swedish</a> males, 40% of <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian people">Norwegian</a> males, and 40% of <a href="/wiki/Danish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Danish people">Danish</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroups</a>. <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_I1" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup I1">Haplogroup I1</a> peaks in certain areas of Northern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and Eastern <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> at more than 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonald2005_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonald2005-326">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Germanic_studies_and_their_influence">Later Germanic studies and their influence</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Viking_revival" title="Viking revival">Viking revival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothicism" title="Gothicism">Gothicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">Scandinavism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">Pan-Germanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/240px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/360px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/480px-M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3063" data-file-height="4430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge_-_Tor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Thor%27s_Fight_with_the_Giants" title="Thor&#39;s Fight with the Giants">Thor's Fight with the Giants</a></i> (1872) by <span lang="sv" style="font-style:normal;" title="Swedish language text"><a href="/wiki/M%C3%A5rten_Eskil_Winge" title="Mårten Eskil Winge">Mårten Eskil Winge</a></span> was made during the <a href="/wiki/Viking_revival" title="Viking revival">Viking revival</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> revived interest in pre-Christian <a href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a> and only in a second phase in pre-Christian Northern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath2015146–151_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath2015146–151-327">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> The Germanic peoples of the Roman era are often lumped with the other agents of the <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarian</a> invasions, the Alans and the Huns, as opposed to the civilized "Roman" identity of the Holy Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns20033–9,_14–23,_331_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns20033–9,_14–23,_331-328">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early modern publications dealing with <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> culture appeared in the 16th century, e.g. <i>Historia de gentibus septentrionalibus</i> (Olaus Magnus, 1555) and the first edition of the 13th century <i><a href="/wiki/Gesta_Danorum" title="Gesta Danorum">Gesta Danorum</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Saxo_Grammaticus" title="Saxo Grammaticus">Saxo Grammaticus</a>), in 1514.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGolther19083_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGolther19083-329">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Authors of the <a href="/wiki/German_Renaissance" title="German Renaissance">German Renaissance</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Aventinus" title="Johannes Aventinus">Johannes Aventinus</a> discovered the <i>Germanii</i> of Tacitus as the "Old Germans", whose virtue and unspoiled manhood, as it appears in the Roman accounts of <a href="/wiki/Noble_savage" title="Noble savage">noble savagery</a>, they contrast with the decadence of their own day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrauss1963229–230_330-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrauss1963229–230-330">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pace of publication increased during the 17th century with Latin translations of the <a href="/wiki/Edda" title="Edda">Edda</a> (notably Peder Resen's <i>Edda Islandorum</i> of 1665). The <a href="/wiki/Viking_revival" title="Viking revival">Viking revival</a> of 18th century <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> created a fascination with anything "Nordic" in disposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMjöberg1980207–238_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMjöberg1980207–238-331">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> The beginning of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_philology" title="Germanic philology">Germanic philology</a> proper begins in the early 19th century, with <a href="/wiki/Rasmus_Rask" title="Rasmus Rask">Rasmus Rask</a>'s <i>Icelandic Lexicon </i> of 1814, and was in full bloom by the 1830s, with <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Grimm" title="Jacob Grimm">Jacob Grimm</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mythologie" title="Deutsche Mythologie">Deutsche Mythologie</a></i> giving an extensive account of reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Germanic_mythology" title="Germanic mythology">Germanic mythology</a> and composing a German dictionary (<i><a href="/wiki/Deutsches_W%C3%B6rterbuch" title="Deutsches Wörterbuch">Deutsches Wörterbuch</a></i>) of <a href="/wiki/Common_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Germanic">Germanic etymology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911912_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911912-332">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Jacob Grimm also coauthored with his brother <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Grimm" title="Wilhelm Grimm">Wilhelm</a>, the famous <a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_Fairy_Tales" class="mw-redirect" title="Grimm&#39;s Fairy Tales">Grimm's Fairy Tales</a>. Apart from linguistic studies, the subject of what became of the Roman era Germanic tribes, and how they influenced the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> was a subject discussed during <a href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="The Enlightenment">the Enlightenment</a> by such as writers as <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_Vico" title="Giambattista Vico">Giambattista Vico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKramerMaza2002124–138_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKramerMaza2002124–138-333">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later still, the development of Germanic studies as an academic discipline in the 19th century ran parallel to the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> in Europe and the search for <a href="/wiki/Nationalism_and_historiography" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalism and historiography">national histories</a> for the nascent <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a> developing after the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">end</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2011242–243_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2011242–243-334">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> A "Germanic" national ethnicity offered itself for the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">unification of Germany</a>, contrasting the emerging <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> with its neighboring rivals of differing ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJansen2011242–249_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJansen2011242–249-335">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> The nascent belief in a <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German ethnicity</a> was subsequently founded upon <a href="/wiki/National_myth" title="National myth">national myths</a> of Germanic antiquity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMosse196467–87_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMosse196467–87-336">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> These tendencies culminated in a later <a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">Pan-Germanism</a>, <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Alldeutsche Bewegung</i></i> which had as its aim, the political unity of all of <a href="/wiki/German-speaking_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="German-speaking Europe">German-speaking Europe</a> (all <i><a href="/wiki/Volksdeutsche" title="Volksdeutsche">Volksdeutsche</a></i>) into a Teutonic nation state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMosse1964218–225_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMosse1964218–225-337">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith198997–111_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith198997–111-338">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">Romantic nationalism</a> in Scandinavia placed more weight on the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, resulting in the movement known as <a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">Scandinavism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDerry201227,_220,_238–248_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDerry201227,_220,_238–248-339">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">theories of race</a> developed in the same period, which used Darwinian evolutionary ideals and <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudo-scientific</a> methods in the identification of Germanic peoples (members of a <a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic race</a>), as being superior to other ethnicities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeikart20063–10,_102–126_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeikart20063–10,_102–126-340">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Scientific racism flourished in the late 19th century and into the mid-20th century, where it became the basis for specious racial comparisons and justification for eugenic efforts; it also contributed to compulsory sterilization, anti-miscegenation laws, and was used to sanction immigration restrictions in both Europe and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;ba&#93;</a></sup> Following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, as a response to political influences of the past, government support for the study of ancient Germanic history and culture was significantly reduced both in Germany and Scandinavia.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;bb&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As late as the 10th century there is evidence of runic writing on a stone monument erected by the first Christian king of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harald_Bluetooth" title="Harald Bluetooth">Harald Bluetooth</a>. In the text, Harald honors his parents using runic script and on the other side of the stone is a depiction of 'Christ in His Glory', incorporating a runic inscription which extolls Harald for acquiring Denmark and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and for converting the <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danes</a> into Christians. See: Moltke (1985). <i>Runes and Their Origin: Denmark and Elsewhere</i>, pp. 207–220.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Constantine credited his victories against Maxentius in 311–312 principally to his barbarian troops, who were honoured on the triumphal Arch of Constantine in Rome. In opposition to him, Licinius mustered drafts of Goths to strengthen his army. Goths were also brought in by Constantine, to the number of 40,000, it is said, to help defend Constantinople in the latter part of his reign, and the palace guard was thenceforward composed mostly of Germans, from among whom a great many high army commands were filled. Dependence on immigrants or first-generation barbarians in war was to increase steadily, at a time when conventional Roman troops were losing military value."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Germanic peoples were the scourge of the Western Empire. Nevertheless, it was only with German help that the empire was able to survive as long as it did. The Roman army received an ever-growing number of recruits from the German tribes..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Europe:_The_Germans_and_Huns,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Europe:_The_Germans_and_Huns,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> </span>

<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Recent academic work from the likes of Peter Heather supports this argument. (See: Heather, Peter. (2012) <i>Empires and Barbarians: The Fall of Rome and the Birth of Europe</i>). Conversely, historian <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Ward-Perkins" title="Bryan Ward-Perkins">Bryan Ward-Perkins</a> paints a different picture altogether. Ward-Perkins states that, "The invaders were not guilty of murder, but they had committed manslaughter." (See: Ward-Perkins, (2005) <i>The Fall of Rome: And the End of Civilization</i>, p. 134.) The two titles alone speak to their divergent positions.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-178"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-178">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"So much for the conventional notions. In real life, these tribes were surprisingly small: fifteen to twenty thousand warriors—which means a total of about one hundred thousand people in a tribe—was the maximum number a large people could raise. In defiance of the facts, we hear to this day of barbarian hordes. These people are likewise presented as conquerors of the Roman Empire, even though they constituted a vanishing minority within it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram19977_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram19977-177">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The barbarians were everywhere a small minority. They established themselves on the great estates and divided the land to the benefit of the federates without doing much harm to the lower classes or disturbing the economy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAncient_Rome:_The_Barbarian_Invasions,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> </span>

The majority of the population, probably about six million, were Hispano-Romans, as compared with 200,000 barbarians."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpain:_Visigothic_Spain_to_c._500,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpain:_Visigothic_Spain_to_c._500,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-180">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> </span>

<li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[H]is people could not legally intermarry with Romans."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheodoric,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheodoric,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-182">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> </span>

<li id="cite_note-184"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-184">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Visigothic king was theoretically ruler of only his own people, whereas the Hispano-Romans continued to profess allegiance to a rapidly vanishing imperial authority. A Roman law that prohibited intermarriage between the two peoples was, however, abolished in the late 6th century."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpain:_Visigothic_Spain_to_c._500,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpain:_Visigothic_Spain_to_c._500,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-180">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-193"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-193">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a period of upwards of 1,300 years since the Frankish king Clovis was converted to Christianity (he ruled Gaul in what eventually became modern France), eighteen monarchs of France have been Christened with a French derivation of his Latin name <i>Ludovicus</i> or "Louis" in modern French. See: Diarmaid MacCulloch, <i>Christianity: The First Three Thousand Years</i> (New York: Penguin, 2011), p. 324.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"During Roman times. Germanic peoples arrived from the east conquering whatever Celtic lands the Romans had not taken, namely the areas just north of the Danube and east of the Rhine. These tribes continually threatened the Roman Empire, sacking Rome itself for the first time in a.d. 410. By the end of the 400s. Gaul was taken over by the Franks, eventually to be renamed for them (France). The Burgundians lent their name to a province (Burgundy) that was eventually absorbed into France. The Visigoths and Lombards moved into the Italian peninsula. The latter name is found in the modern Italian provincial name of Lombardy. The Angles and Saxons moved into the British Isles, pushing the Celtic peoples farther into the fringes of Europe. Even today, the English are considered Anglo-Saxons. Other Germanic tribes moved north into Scandinavia. By the a.d. 800s, they developed a distinct Viking culture... Germanic culture is still prevalent today. Though the Franks, Burgundians. and Lombards adopted the Romance languages of the Roman provinces they conquered, other Germanic peoples, like the Vikings, maintained their Germanic languages through the centuries and are clearly seen on the map today (see Figure 3.4a). Germans. Austrians, Dutch, and the Scandinavians (such as Danes, Norwegians, and Swedes) are the most numerous of today's Germanic peoples. The Germanic peoples also converted to Christianity and later became the driving force behind the creation of the branch of Christianity known as Protestantism."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBradshawWhiteDymondChacko200772_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBradshawWhiteDymondChacko200772-213">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Germanic peoples (3A). A broadly defined group of peoples from northern Europe who began to move south into Germany and other areas of Europe around 500 B.C. Modern Germans, Austrians, Dutch, and the Scandinavians (Danes, Norwegians, Swedes) are the most numerous of today's Germanic peoples."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBradshawWhiteDymondChacko2007572_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBradshawWhiteDymondChacko2007572-215">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Germanic... is a collective term referring to the peoples who speak the modern Germanic languages, Swedes, Danes, Norwegians, Icelanders, English, Frisians, Dutch and Germans, and to the ancestors of these peoples."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPasleyBithell19725_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPasleyBithell19725-217">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Germanic... group of N European peoples including the Germans, Scandinavians, Dutch, English, etc., or the peoples from whom they are descended."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic,_&#39;&#39;Webster’s_New_World_College_Dictionary&#39;&#39;_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic,_&#39;&#39;Webster’s_New_World_College_Dictionary&#39;&#39;-219">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"When a nation either defends itself in war or wages it, magistrates are selected to be in charge of the war with power of life and death... Those unwilling to follow are thought as deserters and traitors and are no longer trusted in anything."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar2019155,_6.23_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar2019155,_6.23-222">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> </span>

<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">E.g. reduction of the weregild to half the regular amount if the man responsible for the killing is employed by the king in the laws of Æthelberht of Kent, paragraph 7.</span>

<li id="cite_note-244"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-244">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Warriors were physically adept and owed much of their <i><a href="/wiki/Esprit_de_corps" class="mw-redirect" title="Esprit de corps">esprit de corps</a></i> to the loyalty existing between themselves and their tribal chieftains. After forming a shield wall, they would then hurl a single spear in unison as a sacrifice to Odin. Fighting thereafter normally devolved to a gang raid and individual combat. See: Waldman &amp; Mason 2006, p. 837.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-248"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-248">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This and the following information is based on P.J. Geary, <i>Before France and Germany. The Creation and Transformation of the Merovingian World</i> (New York: Oxford University Press, 1988), 44 ff. and M. Innes, <i>Introduction to Early Medieval Western Europe, 300–900</i> (Abingdon 2007), 71–72.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Teutonic tribes who moved into a large part of western Europe did not come empty-handed, and in some respects their technology was superior to that of the Romans. It has already been observed that they were people of the Iron Age, and although much about the origins of the heavy plow remains obscure these tribes appear to have been the first people with sufficiently strong iron plowshares to undertake the systematic settlement of the forested lowlands of northern and western Europe, the heavy soils of which had frustrated the agricultural techniques of their predecessors. The invaders came thus as colonizers. They may have been regarded as “barbarians” by the Romanized inhabitants of western Europe who naturally resented their intrusion, and the effect of their invasion was certainly to disrupt trade, industry, and town life. But the newcomers also provided an element of innovation and vitality."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Technology:_Medieval_advance_(500–1500_ce),_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHistory_of_Technology:_Medieval_advance_(500–1500_ce),_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-255">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-258"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-258">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Teutonic tribes who conquered and divided the Roman Empire were little versed in the monumental arts and unskilled in figure representation; but in metalworking, in the making of weapons and other utilitarian objects, and in the delicate ornament of the goldsmith’s art they excelled.."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetalwork:_Teutonic_Tribes,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetalwork:_Teutonic_Tribes,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-257">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-260"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-260">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Some smiths were able to rework iron into high-quality steel and make sword blades with a core of softer steel for flexibility and harder steel on the exterior to keep a sharp edge, far finer weapons than those used in the Roman army at the time."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006324_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaldmanMason2006324-259">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-262"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-262">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Furthermore, the skills of Germanic smiths and other craftsmen were as good as, or better than those found inside the Roman empire."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDowall200016_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDowall200016-261">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-264"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-264">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[T]hey allowed import of no wine or other luxury goods, because they thought these things weakned spirits and diminshed courage..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar201951,_2.15_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar201951,_2.15-263">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-266"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-266">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"They do not let wine be imported to them, for they think it softens men for hard work and makes them womanly."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaesar201951,_4.2_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaesar201951,_4.2-265">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-282"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-282">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See: Young, Bruce W. (2008). <i>Family Life in the Age of Shakespeare</i>. Greenwood Press, pp. 16–17.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-303"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-303">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Many groups of Germanic peoples shared one form or another of a creation story where a divine being emerges from nothingness only to be sacrificed and torn to pieces; the bones of this divine creature (named Ymir) produced the rocks, his flesh became the earth, his blood formed the seas, the clouds emerged from his hair, and his skull made up the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade1984155–156_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade1984155–156-301">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In this creation story, a mighty tree called <i><a href="/wiki/Yggdrasill" class="mw-redirect" title="Yggdrasill">Yggdrasill</a></i> is situated at the center of the earth, its top touching the sky, its branches covering the earth, and the great tree's roots plunging into hell. Connecting the three planes of "Heaven, Earth, and Hades", this "Universal Tree" symbolized the universe itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade1984157_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade1984157-302">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-305"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-305">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The principle shared deity among the Germanic tribes, Odin-Wodan, (in varying name forms) was not only the god of war, but of the dead as well. Odin-Wodan protected great heroes in combat but often killed his "protégés", who were led to him by the Valkyries and gathered together to practice fighting in preparation for the final eschatological battle of the <i>Ragnarök</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEliade1984161_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEliade1984161-304">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-306"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-306">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The rigorous ethics of early Germanic society, based on trust, loyalty, and courage, and the perhaps somewhat idealized picture of the moral code given by Tacitus, had a divine sanction..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGermanic_religion_and_mythology,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGermanic_religion_and_mythology,_&#39;&#39;Encyclopædia_Britannica_Online&#39;&#39;-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-319"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-319">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> See: Levison (1905). <i>Vitae Sancti Bonifatii archiepiscopi moguntini</i>, pp. 31–32.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-323"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-323">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">New Phylthatetic Relationships for Y-chromosome Haplogroup I: Reappraising its Phylogeography and Prehistory," Rethinking the Human Evolution, Mellars P, Boyle K, Bar-Yosef O, Stringer C, Eds. McDonald Institute for Archaeological Research, Cambridge, UK, 2007, pp. 33–42 by Underhill PA, Myres NM, Rootsi S, Chow CT, Lin AA, Otillar RP, King R, Zhivotovsky LA, Balanovsky O, Pshenichnov A, Ritchie KH, Cavalli-Sforza LL, Kivisild T, Villems R, Woodward SR.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-341"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-341">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For more on the historical trek of European anti-Semitism and how scientific racism contributed to the Holocaust, see: Mosse, George L. <i>Toward the Final Solution: A History of European Racism</i>. New York: Harper &amp; Row, 1980.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-343"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-343">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In Germany...the first need was to detach prehistoric studies from the political influences of the pre-war period. German archaeologists, like their Scandinavian colleagues though sometimes for different reasons, have had to make do with very slender financial resources."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOxenstierna19673_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOxenstierna19673-342">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
